The Fifth Argument: Obligating the Foreknown Unbeliever
Al-Razi presents the fifth argument concerning the futility of obligating those God knows will disbelieve.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against divine obligation: that obligating someone God knows will disbelieve leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Obligating a known future disbeliever either implies divine ignorance or leads to unjust punishment.

Al-Razi's fifth argument targets the fairness of obligation given divine foreknowledge. If God obligates a person whom He knows will reject faith, then either the obligated act occurs (implying God's knowledge was wrong) or it does not (making the person deserve punishment). Both options are unacceptable for a wise God.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument
Find the section beginning with 'الخامس' and read it carefully. Note the two possible outcomes and their implications.
The argument is concise but powerful.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
Al-Razi presents a dilemma: if the act occurs, God is ignorant (since He knew it wouldn't); if the act doesn't occur, the person is punished without benefit. Both are problematic.
This challenges the justice and omniscience of God if obligation exists.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'تجهيل' (making ignorant)
The term 'تجهيل' refers to attributing ignorance to God. If the obligated act comes to pass contrary to God's knowledge, it would make God ignorant, which is impossible.
4
Connect to the overall doubt
This argument builds on the previous ones by showing that obligation leads to logical or moral absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
تقرير
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.
السادس : أن التكليف يقتضي شغل القلب بتلك الأعمال، واشتغال القلب بغير يمنعه عن الاستغراق في معرفة الله ومحبته، وكل ما كان مانعا من معرفة الله ومحبته كان تركه من أوجب الواجبات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة أن القول بالتكليف باطل، وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بالبعثة أيضًا باطلا؛ لأنه لا فائدة من البعثة إلا توجيه التكاليف على الخلق، وإذا كان المقصود باطلا كان التبع أولى بالبطلان.
The Thirtieth Issue: The case of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the case of equality, it is impossible because the action would be preponderant, and obtaining preponderance while equality obtains is a conjunction of contradictories. As for the case of preponderance, it is impossible because the preponderant is necessary and the inferior is impossible—according to what we have established of this premise in other places. If obligation (taklif) falls upon the preponderant, it is an obligation to bring about something necessarily occurring; and if it falls upon the inferior, it is an obligation to bring about something impossible of occurrence. The completion of this point has preceded in the issue of the creation of acts. Summary: Fourth: Obligation has absolutely no benefit, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. We say it has no benefit because if that benefit redounds to the Worshipped, it would necessitate that He is subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it redounds to the worshipper, it is also false, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure and joy, or what leads to them, or the repulsion of pain or grief, or what leads to them. And the Worshipped, exalted, is able to achieve all of that for the servant without the intermediary of obligation, so the intermediation of obligation is futility. If this is established, it is clear that futility is not befitting the Most Wise of judges. Fifth: Obligating one whom He knows will disbelieve or act wickedly is unbefitting of wisdom, because if the obligated act comes into existence, it necessitates ignorance of the Worshipped; and if it does not come into existence, it necessitates the servant's deserving punishment. Doing something with no benefit except one of these two objectionable things is not befitting of wisdom. Sixth: Obligation necessitates occupying the heart with those acts, and occupying the heart with other than [God] prevents it from absorption in knowledge of God and love of Him. Anything that prevents knowledge of God and love of Him, its abandonment is among the most obligatory duties. Thus, by these six aspects it is established that the claim of obligation is false. If this is established, the claim of sending messengers is also false, because there is no benefit from sending messengers except directing obligations upon creation. If the purpose is false, the subordinate is more deserving of falsehood.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt of those who deny prophethood. It consists of six rational arguments against divine obligation (taklif). The passage begins with a discussion of the preponderance of motives: if a motive is preponderant, the act becomes necessary; if motives are equal, the act is impossible. Thus obligation is either impossible or unnecessary. Then three numbered arguments follow. The fourth argument claims obligation has no benefit because God can directly grant all pleasures and remove all pains without it, so obligation is futile. The fifth argument points out that obligating someone God knows will disbelieve or sin leads either to God being ignorant (if the act occurs) or to punishment (if it doesn't), both unbefitting wisdom. The sixth argument says obligation distracts the heart from knowledge and love of God, which is the most important duty. The conclusion is that since obligation is impossible, sending prophets (whose purpose is to convey obligation) is also impossible.

Key Terms
تجهيل (making ignorant)
عقاب (punishment)
حكمة (wisdom)
كفر (disbelief)
فسق (wickedness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the outcome if the obligated act of a foreknown disbeliever comes into existence?
A) God is shown to be just
B) It necessitates ignorance of God
C) The servant is rewarded
D) It demonstrates wisdom

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that obligating those God knows will reject faith leads to either divine ignorance or pointless punishment, both unbefitting wisdom.

The Sixth Argument: Distraction from Knowledge and Love of God
Al-Razi presents the sixth argument that obligation distracts from knowledge and love of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against divine obligation: that obligation occupies the heart with other than God, preventing the highest good of knowledge and love of Him.

Core Idea
Obligation distracts from the ultimate purpose of knowing and loving God.

Al-Razi's sixth and final argument in this doubt claims that religious obligations preoccupy the heart with acts instead of God. Since the highest goal is absorption in knowledge and love of God, anything that prevents this should be abandoned. Therefore, obligation becomes a hindrance rather than a help.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth argument
Find the section starting with 'السادس' and read it. Note how al-Razi prioritizes knowledge and love of God.
This argument appeals to spiritual values.
2
Identify the core claim
Obligation, by requiring acts, occupies the heart. This occupation prevents the heart from being absorbed in knowledge and love of God.
3
Understand the normative conclusion
Since knowledge and love of God are the most obligatory duties, anything that prevents them should be abandoned. Hence, obligation itself should be abandoned.
This is a radical conclusion: obligations are counterproductive.
4
Connect to the overall doubt conclusion
This argument completes the doubt. Al-Razi then concludes that if obligation is false, prophethood (which conveys obligation) is also false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
تقرير
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.

The Thirtieth Issue: The case of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the case of equality, it is impossible because the action would be preponderant, and obtaining preponderance while equality obtains is a conjunction of contradictories. As for the case of preponderance, it is impossible because the preponderant is necessary and the inferior is impossible—according to what we have established of this premise in other places. If obligation (taklif) falls upon the preponderant, it is an obligation to bring about something necessarily occurring; and if it falls upon the inferior, it is an obligation to bring about something impossible of occurrence. The completion of this point has preceded in the issue of the creation of acts. Summary: Fourth: Obligation has absolutely no benefit, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. We say it has no benefit because if that benefit redounds to the Worshipped, it would necessitate that He is subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it redounds to the worshipper, it is also false, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure and joy, or what leads to them, or the repulsion of pain or grief, or what leads to them. And the Worshipped, exalted, is able to achieve all of that for the servant without the intermediary of obligation, so the intermediation of obligation is futility. Key Terms
معرفة الله (knowledge of God)
محبة الله (love of God)
شغل القلب (occupation of heart)
استغراق (absorption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what is the most obligatory duty?
A) Performing the obligation
B) Abandoning whatever prevents knowledge and love of God
C) Seeking worldly benefit
D) Avoiding punishment

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument holds that obligation distracts from the highest goal of knowing and loving God, so avoiding such distraction is itself the most obligatory duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one obligatory act you perform regularly. In the next performance, observe your heart: is it truly absorbed in God, or is it occupied by the act itself? If the latter, what would it take for you to shift from duty to direct love?
?
Al-Razi suggests that anything that prevents knowledge and love of God must be abandoned, even if it is normally obligatory. Is there anything in your religious life—a habit, a practice, a routine—that you suspect might be distracting you from God rather than drawing you near? Are you willing to leave it for His sake?
?
If you were to spend one hour today without any prescribed worship, simply trying to know and love God through stillness and thought, would your heart feel empty or full? What does your discomfort reveal about your reliance on actions rather than on Him?

Prophethood

The Second Doubt: Moral Knowledge by Reason Alone
Al-Razi records the claim that all moral judgments are known by reason, making prophethood needless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that claims reason alone can judge all actions, thereby rendering prophethood unnecessary.

Core Idea
If reason knows all moral judgments, prophethood becomes futile.

Al-Razi presents the second objection from those who deny prophethood. They argue that every action's moral status is either known by reason to be good, known to be bad, or unknown. In the unknown case, reason can still determine whether to do it or not. Thus, there is no need for a divine messenger to inform us of moral duties. This passage challenges the necessity of prophethood based on the sufficiency of human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a logical division here.
2
Identify the tripartite division of actions
The passage divides actions into three categories: those whose goodness is known, those whose badness is known, and those whose moral status is unknown. Find these three in the text.
This sets up the structure of the argument.
3
Analyze the treatment of unknown actions
For unknown actions, the argument subdivides them into necessary and non-necessary. Understand how reason handles each subcategory.
Necessary actions must be done; non-necessary actions must be avoided due to potential harm.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that since all actions are covered by reason (either known or determined by reason), there is no need for a messenger. Sending one would be futile.
This directly challenges the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الثانية لمنكري النبوات أن الأفعال إما أن يكون حسنها معلوما، أو يكون قبحها معلوما ، أو لا نعلم لا حسنها ولا قبحها، فإن كان حسنها معلومًا عملناه، ولا حاجة بنا في ذلك إلى تعريف الشرع، وإن كان قبحها معلوما تركناه، وإن لم نعلم لا حسنها ولا قبحها فهذا الشيء إما أن يكون فعله اضطراريًا كالتنفس في الهواء، وما يجري مجراه وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان فعله اضطراريًا كان جورًا لا محالة، لأن تكليف ما لا يطاق لا يليق بالحكمة، وإن لم يكن فعله اضطراريًا وجب علينا تركه ؛ لأن مثل هذا الشيء لا حاجة بنا إلى فعله، وفي فعله خطر، واحتمال ضرر، وكل ما كان كذلك اقتضى العقل تركه.
فثبت بما ذكرنا أن جملة أحكام الأفعال في الفعل والترك معلومة لنا، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن في بعثة الرسول فائدة، فتكون البعثة عبثا والعبث غير جائز على الحكيم.
The second doubt of the deniers of prophethood: Actions are either such that their goodness is known, or their badness is known, or we know neither their goodness nor their badness. If their goodness is known, we do it, and we have no need in that for definition by the Law. If their badness is known, we abandon it. If we know neither their goodness nor badness, then this thing is either such that its doing is necessary, like breathing air and the like, or it is not like that. If its doing is necessary, it is injustice undoubtedly, because obligating what cannot be borne is not befitting wisdom. If its doing is not necessary, then we must abandon it, because we have no need to do such a thing, and in doing it there is danger and possibility of harm. Everything that is such requires reason to abandon it. Thus, it is established by what we have mentioned that all the judgments of actions concerning doing and abandoning are known to us. If that is the case, there is no benefit in sending a messenger, so sending a messenger is futility, and futility is not permissible for the Wise.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the second doubt of the deniers of prophethood. It argues that all moral judgments about actions—whether they are good, bad, or neutral—are known by reason alone, so there is no need for divine revelation. The argument divides actions into three categories: those known to be good (we do them), those known to be bad (we avoid them), and those whose moral status is unknown. For unknown actions, if they are necessary (like breathing), we must do them; if they are not necessary, reason requires avoiding them due to potential harm. Since all actions are covered by reason, sending a messenger to declare what is already known is futile.

Key Terms
حسن (goodness)
قبح (badness)
اضطراري (necessary)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (obligation of what cannot be borne)
شرع (Law)
بعثة (sending)
عبث (futility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second doubt?
A) Prophethood is necessary because we need divine guidance for moral knowledge
B) Prophethood is impossible because all actions' judgments are known by reason alone
C) Prophethood is possible but not needed
D) Prophethood is required for necessary actions

Key Takeaway
The second doubt argues that reason alone can judge all actions, thereby eliminating the need for prophethood and rendering it futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you rely on your own reasoning to decide right from wrong without consulting Allah’s revelation? Does this lesson expose a subtle arrogance in your heart that thinks you don’t need divine guidance?
?
If you truly believed that reason alone is sufficient for all moral knowledge, would your daily relationship with the Quran change? Are you willing to admit that your intellect has limits and that you desperately need revelation?
?
The doubt claims that prophethood is futile if reason knows everything. But when you face a real moral dilemma where your reason is confused, do you turn to the Prophet’s guidance? Or do you trust your own judgment and ignore what Allah has sent?

The Third Doubt: Irrationality of Religious Practices
Al-Razi records the objection that religious rituals are arbitrary and irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt of the deniers of prophethood, which claims that religious practices like Hajj and the distinction between similar days are irrational and arbitrary.

Core Idea
The third doubt claims religious rituals are arbitrary, exemplified by similar days having opposite rulings.

Al-Razi presents a critical objection against prophethood: that religious laws contain actions that seem irrational. In this passage, he records the claim that rituals like Hajj and the distinction between similar days lack wisdom. Let's examine the doubt and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi is presenting an objection, not his own view.
2
Identify the two main examples
The passage gives two examples of irrationality: the rituals of Hajj (specifying a particular house for veneration) and the distinction between the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal (similar days with opposite rulings). Find these in the text.
The second example is more detailed.
3
Reflect on the claim of arbitrariness
The doubt argues that these practices are 'not befitting wisdom' because they assign special significance to things without any apparent reason. Consider why this is a challenge to prophethood.
The response to this doubt will come in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة لمنكري النبوات : أنا نشاهد في الشرائع أفعالا غير لائقة بالحكمة، مثل أعمال الحج ومثل إيقاع الفرق بين المتشابهات، فإنهم خصصوا بيتا معينًا لغاية التعظيم من غير سبب، وخصصوا أوقاتا معينة لعبادات معينة، مع أن سائر الأوقات مساوية لها، وذلك لأن اليوم الأخير من رمضان واليوم الأول من شوال يومان متلاصقان متشابهان من جميع الوجوه، ثم خصصوا أحدهما بحرمة الإفطار والآخر بحرمة الصوم، وذكروا من أمثال هذه المسائل شيئًا كثيرا.
The third doubt of the deniers of prophethood: We observe in the religious laws actions not befitting wisdom, such as the rituals of Hajj and making a distinction between similar things. They specified a particular house for extreme veneration without reason, and specified particular times for particular acts of worship, while other times are equivalent to them. For example, the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal are two consecutive days similar in all respects, yet they specified one with the prohibition of breaking the fast and the other with the prohibition of fasting. They mentioned many instances of such issues.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt of the deniers of prophethood. It claims that certain religious practices are irrational and arbitrary. Examples include specifying the Kaaba for worship, designating particular times for rituals, and distinguishing between days like the last day of Ramadan (when fasting is obligatory) and the first day of Shawwal (when fasting is prohibited) despite them being similar in all intrinsic respects. The doubt argues that such assignments without external reason are unbefitting of a wise lawgiver.

Key Terms
شريعة (religious law)
حج (Hajj)
تعظيم (veneration)
عبادات (acts of worship)
حرمة (prohibition)
صوم (fasting)
إفطار (breaking fast)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does this doubt use to claim irrationality in religious laws?
A) The distinction between Makkah and Madinah
B) The distinction between the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal
C) The obligation of prayer five times a day
D) The prohibition of eating pork

Key Takeaway
The third doubt attacks the wisdom of religious law by pointing to apparent arbitrariness in rituals and temporal distinctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself reluctant to obey a command of Allah because it seemed irrational or without clear benefit? What does that reluctance reveal about your trust in His wisdom? This week, commit to obeying one such command purely out of submission, without needing to understand the reason.
?
The doubt criticizes the distinction between the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal. When you celebrate Eid, do you feel the shift from fasting to feasting is merely a human tradition, or do you recognize it as a divinely ordained transition that shapes your relationship with Allah? How does your heart respond to the fact that this distinction is based solely on revelation?
?
Imagine you are asked to defend the Hajj rituals to a skeptic. Can you? If not, does your inability weaken your own faith? Today, reflect on whether your acceptance of these rituals is rooted in a conviction that Allah's commands are always wise, even when hidden from you. Pray for the strength to submit fully.

The Jewish Doubt Against Abrogation
Al-Razi presents the Jewish doubt and refutes it via a trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish argument that abrogation is impossible and Al-Razi's refutation using the trilemma of Moses' declaration.

Core Idea
A prophet's law must be declared eternal; otherwise, abrogation is logically impossible.

This passage presents a key Jewish doubt that attempts to invalidate the prophethood of Muhammad by arguing that abrogation is impossible. Al-Razi systematically examines the three possibilities regarding what Moses communicated about his law's duration, showing that only the claim of eternity is consistent, thereby refuting the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Jewish doubt
Read the passage orientation and the first lines describing the doubt: the Jews claim abrogation is invalid, so Muhammad's law cannot be valid.
This doubt targets the very concept of abrogation.
2
Analyze the three possibilities
Work through the trilemma: Did Moses declare his law eternal, temporary, or remain silent? Al-Razi argues that only 'eternal' is consistent.
Focus on the reasons for rejecting the other two options.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Understand why Al-Razi concludes that Moses must have declared his law eternal, making any later abrogation a lie. Thus the Jewish doubt fails.
This conclusion refutes the doubt by showing its internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة لمنكري النبوات - وهي شبهة اليهود أن النسخ باطل، وإذا كان كذلك كان شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم باطلا.
وإنما قلنا : إن النسخ باطل لوجوه :
الأول : أن موسى لما بلغ شرعه إلى أمته فإما أن يقال: إنه بين أن شرعه مؤبد أو يقال : إنه بين أن شرعه منقطع، أو لم يبين لا هذا ولا ذاك.
لا جائز أن يقال : إنه ال بين أن شرعه منقطع، وذلك لأنه لو وشرحه لأمته لوجب أن يصير ذلك معلومًا بالتواتر فيلزم أن يكون العلم بكون شرعه منقطعا مساويًا للعلم بأصل شرعه، ولو كان كذلك لما قدر اليهود على إنكار هذا المعنى ؛ لأن ما ثبت بالتواتر لا يمكن إنكاره، ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك لأن اليهود مع اختلافهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها اتفقوا على أن الأمر ليس كذلك. ولا جائز أيضًا أن يقال : إنه بين ذلك الشرع ولم يبين أنه دائم، أو منقطع ؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لما وجب بمقتضى شرعه شيء من الأعمال إلا مرة واحدة، لأن مقتضى الأمر المطلق هو الفعل مرة واحدة لا التكرار، وبالإجماع هذا باطل، ولما بطل هذان القسمان تعين القسم الأول، وهو أنه الله لما بلغ شرعه إلى أنه بین دائم، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون شرعه دائما، وإلا لزم نسبة الكذب إليه، وإنه باطل.
The fourth doubt of the deniers of prophethood—and it is the doubt of the Jews—is that abrogation (naskh) is invalid. If that is so, then the law of Muhammad, peace be upon him, is invalid. We say that abrogation is invalid for several reasons: First: When Moses conveyed his law to his nation, either it is said that he made clear that his law is eternal, or it is said that he made clear that his law is temporary, or he did not clarify either. It is not permissible to say that he made clear that his law is temporary, because if he did clarify and explain it to his nation, it would necessarily become known by tawatur (continuous transmission), and thus knowledge that his law is temporary would be equal to knowledge of the original law. If that were the case, the Jews would not be able to deny this meaning, because what is established by tawatur cannot be denied. It is known that this is not the case, because the Jews, despite their differences in the east and west of the earth, agree that this is not the case. Nor is it permissible to say that he clarified that law but did not clarify whether it is eternal or temporary, because if that were so, nothing of actions would be obligatory by virtue of his law except once, since the requirement of an absolute command is one action, not repetition. By consensus, this is false. Since these two divisions are false, the first division is determined: that when Allah conveyed his law to his nation, he made clear that his law is eternal. If that is so, then his law must be eternal; otherwise, lying would be attributed to him, which is false.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt, specifically the Jewish argument that abrogation is impossible, hence Muhammad's law cannot be valid. The argument examines three possibilities regarding what Moses communicated about his law's duration: that he declared it eternal, that he declared it temporary, or that he did not specify. The first possibility (eternal) is argued to be the only consistent one. The second is rejected because if Moses had declared his law temporary, this would have been transmitted tawatur (universally) and all Jews would know it, which they do not. The third possibility (no specification) is rejected because then actions would only be obligatory once (since absolute command implies one performance), which contradicts Jewish practice. Therefore, Moses must have declared his law eternal, making any later abrogation a lie. Thus Muhammad's law is false.

Key Terms
نسخ (abrogation)
شرع (law)
تواتر (continuous transmission)
أمر (command)
تكرار (repetition)
مؤبد (eternal)
منقطع (temporary)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation is impossible because Moses' law was eternal and cannot be superseded.
Correction: The argument assumes Moses declared his law eternal, but this is not proven; Al-Razi's trilemma shows that the Jews' own consensus refutes the temporary and unspecified options, forcing the eternal option, but the doubt itself is built on a false premise that the eternal declaration is the only logical possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the Jewish doubt as presented by Al-Razi, why can Moses not have declared his law temporary?
A) Because such a declaration would have been transmitted tawatur and known to all Jews
B) Because Moses never made any declarations about duration
C) Because temporary laws are impossible
D) Because the Torah forbids temporary laws

Key Takeaway
The Jewish doubt against abrogation is refuted by showing that the only consistent possibility is that Moses declared his law eternal, which then makes any later abrogation a lie, but this very logic actually undermines the doubt itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief simply because it was what you were taught, refusing to consider that God might have a new directive for you? What would it take for you to admit that your inherited assumptions might be blocking you from a greater truth?
?
The Jewish doubt uses logic to justify rejecting a new prophet. Are you using your own clever reasoning to avoid submitting to something that challenges your comfort? Ask yourself: what is one area of your life where your intellect has become a shield against obedience?
?
If Moses had declared his law temporary, it would have been known by tawatur. Similarly, if there is a truth you need to accept, God would make it clear. But are you actually listening, or have you already decided what the truth must be? Commit today to ask God to show you where you have closed your heart.

Third Objection Against Prophethood: Seemingly Arbitrary Rites
Al-Razi presents the third objection against prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against prophethood that religious rites appear arbitrary and lack apparent wisdom.

Core Idea
What seems arbitrary may stem from hidden divine wisdom.

This lesson introduces the third objection raised by those who deny prophethood. They point to certain religious practices—like the specific house of worship and the distinction between adjacent days—as seemingly arbitrary and unworthy of a wise lawgiver. By reading the passage, you will grasp the structure of this objection and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full sense of the objection.
Notice the tone: the objectors claim the actions are 'not befitting wisdom'.
2
Identify the examples
Find the two main examples given: the specific house (Kaaba) and the specific times (Ramadan/Shawwal).
The objectors use these to argue that the choices seem random.
3
Reflect on the underlying challenge
Consider why this objection challenges prophethood: if a lawgiver appears unwise, why follow the law?
This sets up the need for a defense of divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة لمنكري النبوات : أنا نشاهد في الشرائع أفعالا غير لائقة بالحكمة، مثل أعمال الحج ومثل إيقاع الفرق بين المتشابهات، فإنهم خصصوا بيتا معينًا لغاية التعظيم من غير سبب، وخصصوا أوقاتا معينة لعبادات معينة، مع أن سائر الأوقات مساوية لها، وذلك لأن اليوم الأخير من رمضان واليوم الأول من شوال يومان متلاصقان متشابهان من جميع الوجوه، ثم خصصوا أحدهما بحرمة الإفطار والآخر بحرمة الصوم، وذكروا من أمثال هذه المسائل شيئًا كثيرا.
The third objection of the deniers of prophethood: We observe in the religious laws actions that are not befitting to wisdom, such as the rituals of Hajj and such as creating a distinction between similar things. For they singled out a specific house for utmost veneration without reason, and they singled out specific times for specific acts of worship, while the rest of the times are equivalent to it. That is because the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal are two adjacent days, similar in all respects, yet they singled out one of them for the prohibition of breaking fast and the other for the prohibition of fasting. And they mentioned many such issues.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection of those who deny prophethood. The objectors claim that the religious laws contain actions that are not fitting for a wise lawgiver. They give examples: the specific house of the Kaaba being singled out for veneration, and the arbitrary distinction between similar days. For instance, the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal are adjacent and similar, yet one requires fasting and the other prohibits it. The objection is that such choices appear arbitrary and lack apparent wisdom, thereby questioning the necessity of prophethood. Al-Razi here records the objection to be answered later.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثالثة
النبوات
أعمال الحج
التعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Religious rites appear arbitrary and thus indicate lack of wisdom in the lawgiver.
Correction: The lack of apparent wisdom does not prove actual lack of wisdom; divine wisdom may be hidden.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the third objection against prophethood?
A) That prophethood is unnecessary because reason suffices.
B) That religious rites are arbitrary and show no wisdom.
C) That abrogation is impossible.
D) That Moses declared his law eternal.

Key Takeaway
The third objection against prophethood argues that religious rites like Hajj and distinctions between similar days appear arbitrary, questioning divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a religious command, like the specific rites of Hajj, seems arbitrary? How did you handle that feeling of doubt? What would it mean for your faith if you concluded it were truly arbitrary?
?
Think about the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal—two similar days with opposite rulings. Does this distinction ever stir a quiet question in your heart about why God chose it? How do you respond to that question?

First Proof of the Jewish Objection: The Trilemma
Al-Razi presents the first proof against abrogation via a trilemma on Moses' declaration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of the Jewish objection against abrogation, which argues that Moses must have declared his law eternal, making abrogation impossible.

Core Idea
If Moses declared his law eternal, abrogation would be a lie from God.

This lesson presents Al-Razi's exposition of the Jewish objection against abrogation, focusing on the first proof. The argument uses a trilemma to show that Moses must have declared his law eternal, making abrogation a contradiction of divine truth. We follow the logical steps and see why abrogation is deemed impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure of the trilemma and the eliminations.
Al-Razi uses a very logical, step-by-step approach.
2
Identify the trilemma and elimination
Identify the three options for Moses' declaration (eternal, temporary, unspecified) and see how the objector eliminates two of them using tawatur and consensus.
The elimination of the temporary option relies on the absence of tawatur.
3
Understand the additional argument
Consider the supplementary point: if Moses' eternal declaration could be abrogated, then Muhammad's claim could also be false, destroying trust in all laws.
This extends the argument to a general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة لمنكري النبوات - وهي شبهة اليهود أن النسخ باطل، وإذا كان كذلك كان شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم باطلا. وإنما قلنا : إن النسخ باطل لوجوه : الأول : أن موسى لما بلغ شرعه إلى أمته فإما أن يقال: إنه بين أن شرعه مؤبد أو يقال : إنه بين أن شرعه منقطع، أو لم يبين لا هذا ولا ذاك. لا جائز أن يقال : إنه بين أن شرعه منقطع، وذلك لأنه لو وشرحه لأمته لوجب أن يصير ذلك معلومًا بالتواتر فيلزم أن يكون العلم بكون شرعه منقطعا مساويًا للعلم بأصل شرعه، ولو كان كذلك لما قدر اليهود على إنكار هذا المعنى ؛ لأن ما ثبت بالتواتر لا يمكن إنكاره، ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك لأن اليهود مع اختلافهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها اتفقوا على أن الأمر ليس كذلك. ولا جائز أيضًا أن يقال : إنه بين ذلك الشرع ولم يبين أنه دائم، أو منقطع ؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لما وجب بمقتضى شرعه شيء من الأعمال إلا مرة واحدة، لأن مقتضى الأمر المطلق هو الفعل مرة واحدة لا التكرار، وبالإجماع هذا باطل، ولما بطل هذان القسمان تعين القسم الأول، وهو أنه الله لما بلغ شرعه إلى أنه بین دائم، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون شرعه دائما، وإلا لزم نسبة الكذب إليه، وإنه باطل. وأيضًا فلو جاز فيه أن يقال إنه الا وإن أخبر أن شرعه دائم لكنه لم يدم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن محمدا له أخبر أن شرعه دائم، ثم إنه لا يدوم؟ ومعلوم أن هذا جائز. يوجب زوال الثقة عن جميع الشرائع، وهو باطل.
The fourth objection of the deniers of prophethood—and it is the objection of the Jews that abrogation is invalid. If so, then the law of Muhammad (peace be upon him) would be false. We say that abrogation is invalid for reasons: First: When Moses conveyed his law to his community, it must be said either that he made clear that his law is eternal, or that he made clear that his law is temporary, or he did not clarify either. It is not possible to say that he made clear that his law is temporary, because if he had explained it to his community, it would have become known by tawatur, and knowledge of the temporariness of his law would equal knowledge of the original law. If so, the Jews would not be able to deny this, for what is established by tawatur cannot be denied. It is known that this is not the case, because the Jews, despite their differences across east and west, agree that it is not so. Nor is it possible to say that he clarified the law but did not specify whether it is perpetual or temporary, because if so, nothing from his law would be obligatory except once, since the absolute command requires the action once, not repetition. By consensus, this is false. Since these two possibilities are false, the first is established: that God clarified to his community that his law is eternal. If so, his law must be eternal, otherwise falsehood would be attributed to Him, and that is false. Also, if it were permissible to say that he did so—and he informed that his law is eternal, but it did not endure—then why is it not permissible to say that Muhammad informed that his law is eternal, then it will not endure? It is known that this is possible, and it would cause loss of trust in all laws, and that is false.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Jewish objection against abrogation. The argument begins by stating that when Moses conveyed his law, he must have either (1) declared it eternal, (2) declared it temporary, or (3) left it unspecified. The objector eliminates the second possibility because if Moses had declared his law temporary, it would have been transmitted by tawatur (mass testimony) and would be undeniable, yet Jews universally deny it. The third possibility is eliminated because without specification, the law would only obligate each action once (since absolute commands require only one performance), which is contrary to the established practice. Therefore, only the first possibility remains: Moses declared his law eternal. If so, abrogation would imply that God's declaration was false, which is impossible. An additional argument is added: if it were allowed that Moses declared it eternal yet it was abrogated, then Muhammad's claim that his law is eternal could also be false, undermining trust in all divine laws. Thus, abrogation is impossible.

Key Terms
الشبهة الرابعة
اليهود
النسخ
التواتر
موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that all Jews agree on Moses' intention.
Correction: The argument uses the universal denial by Jews of any temporary declaration as evidence that a tawatur claim is absent; it does not assume their agreement on intention.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof against abrogation, which option is impossible because it would have been transmitted by tawatur?
A) That Moses declared his law eternal.
B) That Moses declared his law temporary.
C) That Moses left his law unspecified.
D) That abrogation is allowed.

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that Moses must have declared his law eternal, making abrogation a contradiction of divine truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the trilemma shows that an eternal law cannot be abrogated without betraying trust, how does your daily indifference to the Quran's commands reflect a hidden doubt about its eternal authority? What one command will you obey from today as if it were your last chance to prove your trust?
?
The argument warns that doubting one eternal declaration destroys trust in all divine laws. Where in your life do you treat Islamic rulings as negotiable while claiming to trust God? What step will you take tonight to close that gap?
?
Moses' law was declared eternal, yet Muslims believe Muhammad's law supersedes it. Does this trilemma trouble you? If not, what resolves it for you? If it does, where must you seek deeper certainty—and when will you start?

Second and Third Proofs Against Abrogation
Al-Razi presents second and third proofs of Jewish objection against abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third proofs of the Jewish objection against abrogation, and how al-Razi refutes them.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not imply badā' in God, and tawatur does not prove eternal laws.

Al-Razi continues presenting the Jewish objections against abrogation. The second proof argues that abrogation implies a change of mind in God, which is impossible. The third proof appeals to the widely transmitted command of Moses regarding the Sabbath. This passage lays out both arguments and sets the stage for al-Razi's responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the second proof carefully. Notice the analogy with a doctor's prescription. Understand the objection and the response.
The objection attempts to liken changes in religious law to medical changes.
2
Examine the refutation
Al-Razi argues that the analogy fails because religious changes lack observable benefit. Understand why this matters.
3
Read the third proof
Read the third proof about the tawatur report from Moses regarding the Sabbath. Recognize the claim that the Sabbath command is eternal.
Tawatur yields certain knowledge in Islamic epistemology.
4
Understand the challenge
If the Sabbath command is eternal, abrogation would contradict Moses' authority. Consider how al-Razi might respond.
This sets up al-Razi's upcoming refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه تعالى لو أمر بشيء ثم نهى عنه لدل ذلك على البداء، وإنه غير جائز. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المصالح تتبدل بحسب اختلاف الأوقات، كالطبيب يأمر المريض بشرب شربة مخصوصة، ثم إنه ينهى عنه بعد ذلك؟ لأنا نقول: هذا إنما يقبل فيما يظهر في تبديله أثر وفائدة كما ذكرتم من تبديل العلاج بالعلاج وههنا ليس كذلك، فإن تحويل العبادة من السبت إلى الجمعة وتحويل القبلة من بيت المقدس إلى الكعبة لا ترى فيه فائدة ولا أثر البتة، فظهر الفرق. الثالث : أن جملة اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها في البلاد ينقلون عن موسى أنه قال : " تمسكوا بالسبت ما دامت السماوات والأرض" وخبر التواتر مفيد للعلم، وقول موسى الحجة، ومتى ثبت هذان الأمران لزم الطعن في شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فهذا مجموع شبهات المنكرين.
Second: If God commanded something and then forbade it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), and that is impermissible. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that benefits change according to different times, like a doctor prescribing a specific drink for a patient and then forbidding it later? For we say: This is acceptable only where an effect and benefit appear in the change, as you mentioned changing treatment for treatment. But here it is not so, for changing worship from Saturday to Friday and changing the qibla from Jerusalem to the Kaaba—no benefit or effect is seen at all in it, so the difference is clear. Third: The entirety of the Jews, with their multitude and dispersion in the east and west of the earth, transmit from Moses that he said: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and earth endure.' And the tawatur report produces knowledge, and the statement of Moses is proof. When these two matters are established, it necessitates criticism of the law of Muhammad (peace be upon him). So this is the totality of the objections of the deniers.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage contains the second and third proofs of the Jewish objection against abrogation. The second proof argues that if God commands something and later forbids it, that implies badā' (a change of mind), which is impermissible for God. An objector might liken this to a doctor changing a prescription, but the response is that medical changes have observable benefits, whereas changes in religious law (like the shift from Saturday to Friday for Sabbath, or from Jerusalem to Mecca for qibla) show no apparent benefit. Thus the analogy fails. The third proof appeals to a widely transmitted report (tawatur) that Moses commanded the Jews to keep the Sabbath 'as long as the heavens and earth endure.' Since tawatur yields certain knowledge, and Moses' word is authoritative, this command would be eternal, and abrogation would invalidate Muhammad's claims. The passage concludes by summarizing these as the totality of the deniers' objections.

Key Terms
البداء
السبت
التواتر
القبلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof is about God's inability to change laws.
Correction: The proof is specifically about implying impermissible change of mind (badā') without apparent reason, not about God's power.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof against abrogation based on?
A) That abrogation would imply badā' (change of mind) in God.
B) That Moses' command about the Sabbath is transmitted by tawatur.
C) That religious laws must always benefit.
D) That Muhammad's law is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Jewish objections rely on the assumptions that abrogation implies badā' and that tawatur from Moses proves eternal law, both of which al-Razi will address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted a command of God simply because you could not see its benefit or wisdom? How does this passage challenge you to trust His wisdom even when no visible effect is apparent?
?
The Jews clung to the Sabbath based on a transmitted tradition. Is there any practice or belief you hold on to merely because it's 'what we've always done'? How can you humbly examine whether God might be calling you to something new?
?
When your plans change unexpectedly, do you ever feel that God has 'changed His mind'? How does the concept of badā' expose your need to trust in God's eternal and unchanging wisdom, rather than your own perception?

Answer to the First Objection: Two Responses
Al-Razi gives two answers to the first objection about the impossibility of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two responses al-Razi gives to the objection that divine obligation (taklīf) is impossible.

Core Idea
Obligation is an informational signal from God, not an arbitrary imposition.

In this passage, al-Razi provides two replies to the objection that divine obligation is impossible. The first exposes a performative contradiction in the objector's own stance, while the second redefines obligation as a divine announcement of reward and punishment, thereby removing the basis for objection. These answers illustrate a key method in kalam: combining dialectical refutation with positive theological definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that al-Razi presents two distinct responses, introduced as 'first' and 'second.'
The second response is described as 'the true answer' (al-jawāb al-ḥaqīqī).
2
Identify the two responses
The first response points out a contradiction in the objector's claim. The second response gives a positive definition of obligation. Identify the key phrases that mark each response.
3
Understand the first response
The objector says 'obligation is false,' but that statement itself is an obligation to believe in its falsity. This is a performative contradiction. Ask yourself: does this argument only work against someone who objects, or does it also apply to the believer?
This is a dialectical move, not the final position.
4
Understand the second response
Al-Razi defines obligation as an announcement (i‘lām) of reward and punishment. Obedience is a sign of reward, disobedience a sign of punishment. No one can question God's assignment of reward and punishment because God is sovereign. Compare this with the objector's assumption that obligation is arbitrary.
This response shifts the discussion from metaphysics to epistemology.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Notice how al-Razi first neutralizes the objection by showing its internal flaw, then provides a constructive definition. This two-step approach is common in kalām. Consider why the first answer alone might be insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجهين : الأول : أن الخصم يقول : " القول بالتكليف باطل وهذا منه تكليف باعتقاد بطلان التكليف، فكان كلامه متناقضا. الثاني - وهو الجواب الحقيقي - أن مذهبنا أن التكليف حاصله يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو أنه إعلام بنزول العقاب أو بنزول الثواب، فإن من صدر عنه الفعل الذي كلف به كان ذلك علامة على نزول الثواب ومن لم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل كان ذلك علامة على نزول العقاب وليس لأحد اعتراض على الله تعالى في أنه لِمَ خصص هذا بالثواب وذلك بالعقاب؟
The answer to the first objection has two aspects: First: The opponent says, 'The doctrine of obligation (taklīf) is false, and this [objection] is itself an obligation to believe in the falsity of obligation, so his speech is contradictory.' Second—and it is the true answer—that our position is that the essence of obligation reduces to a single point: it is an announcement of the descent of punishment or the descent of reward. For if someone performs the act he is obliged to do, that is a sign of the descent of reward, and if he does not perform that act, that is a sign of the descent of punishment. And no one has the right to object to God Almighty as to why He specified this for reward and that for punishment.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage provides the response to the first objection against prophethood. Two answers are given. First, a dialectical refutation: the objector claims that the concept of obligation (taklīf) is false, yet by making this claim he is implicitly obligating others to believe in its falsity, which is a self-contradiction. Second, the substantive answer: al-Razi defines taklīf not as an arbitrary imposition but as an informational signal—God informs people that performing a certain act will lead to reward and neglecting it to punishment. Since God is sovereign, no one can question why He assigns reward and punishment in this way. This answer neutralizes the objection that obligation is pointless or unjust.

Key Terms
التكليف
الثواب
العقاب
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first answer (pointing out contradiction) is a mere sophism and does not address the substance of the objection.
Correction: The first answer is a dialectical refutation that shows the objector's position is self-defeating. It is not intended as the final answer; the second answer provides the substantive response.

Checkpoint
What is the 'true answer' to the first objection according to al-Razi?
A) That obligation is impossible.
B) That obligation is merely information about reward and punishment.
C) That the objector's argument is self-contradictory.
D) That God can do as He wills.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the objection by exposing its self-contradiction and then redefining obligation as a divine announcement of reward and punishment.

?
The objector’s argument is self-defeating—he obligates belief in no obligation. Are there areas in your own life where you hold positions that contradict your actions—for example, claiming something is unimportant while acting as if it is? What will you do to bring consistency?
?
Al-Razi says no one has the right to question God’s specification of reward and punishment. Is there a command of God that you have questioned in your heart? Will you submit to His wisdom and trust that this command is a sign leading to your ultimate good?

Answer to the Second Objection: Reason Needs Emphasis
Al-Razi answers the objection that reason alone suffices, using the principle of emphasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the principle of emphasis to answer the objection that reason alone suffices for guidance.

Core Idea
Prophethood reinforces what reason already knows.

This passage addresses a key objection against prophethood: if reason can independently know God and morality, what need is there for prophets? Al-Razi's response is both subtle and profound. He grants the premise but argues that even repeated messages have value as emphasis and reinforcement — just as God multiplies proofs for tawḥīd in the Qur'an and creation. The lesson unpacks this elegant argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize that the objection claims reason suffices, so prophethood is redundant.
The objection is implicit: if reason can define right and wrong, why send prophets?
2
Grasp Al-Razi's concession
He does not deny reason's sufficiency; he accepts it for the sake of argument ('Grant that reason is sufficient').
This is a rhetorical concession to strengthen his counter-argument.
3
Learn the key move: emphasis
Prophethood adds emphasis (ta'kīd), deepening conviction, just like multiple proofs for tawḥīd.
The analogy shows that additional evidence is beneficial, not redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية فجوابها أن نقول : هب أن العقل كاف في التعريف، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون الفائدة في البعثة تأكيدًا لذاك التعريف؟ ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى من ذكر الدلائل على التوحيد مع أن الواحد منها كاف.
As for the second objection, its answer is that we say: Grant that reason is sufficient for definition, but why is it not permissible that the benefit of sending a messenger is an emphasis (ta'kīd) of that definition? For this reason God Almighty multiplied the evidences for tawḥīd even though one of them is sufficient.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which claimed that prophethood is unnecessary because reason can independently know right and wrong. Al-Razi concedes the premise that reason can provide definition, but argues that prophethood serves as an emphasis (ta'kīd) and reinforcement. He draws an analogy: God has provided multiple proofs for tawḥīd (monotheism) in the Qur'an and creation, even though a single proof would be sufficient. This shows that additional emphasis is not redundant but beneficial for deeper conviction and clarity. Thus, prophethood remains valuable even if reason were sufficient.

Key Terms

Correction: Prophethood's value is not nullified by reason's sufficiency; it serves as reinforcement.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood emphasizes and reinforces rational knowledge, making it more deeply embedded in the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you encounter repeated verses and hadith about the same truth (e.g., the Hereafter), do you dismiss them as repetitive, or do you receive them as gifts of emphasis intended to drive the truth home? Answering Objections: The Role of Rational Good and Evil
Al-Razi gives a conditional answer to the third objection based on rational good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the denial or acceptance of rational good and evil shapes the response to objections regarding seemingly arbitrary divine commands.

Core Idea
The answer to objections about divine commands depends on whether reason can judge good and evil.

In this passage, al-Razi begins his answer to the third objection, which claims that some religious rites appear arbitrary. He immediately ties the answer to one's position on rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-ʻaqliyyayn). For those who deny rational good and evil (the Ash'ari view), the answer is straightforward: God does as He wills. But for those who affirm rational good and evil, a different reasoning is needed. The passage trails off before completing that second answer, but it sets up a crucial distinction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully and note the conditional structure: 'Amma... fa...' (As for... then...). Observe how al-Razi splits the answer based on acceptance or denial of rational good and evil.
The text cuts off mid-sentence, so focus on the structure rather than the complete reasoning.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two scenarios: (a) denial of rational good and evil, and (b) acceptance of rational good and evil. For each, al-Razi gives a different answer. Identify what the answer is for the first scenario and note that the second is incomplete.
The first answer is 'He does what He wills.' The second begins with 'its answer is that the aspect...' but is not finished.
3
Consider the historical context
This debate between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis is central to Islamic theology. Reflect on why the question of rational good and evil matters for understanding divine commands. The fragment shows that the complete answer would continue with a justification even if one accepts rational good and evil.
Understanding the broader debate helps appreciate why al-Razi presents a conditional answer.

The First Objection and Its Refutation
First objection and its two-part answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first objection against abrogation (naskh) and his two-fold answer, including the true nature of taklīf.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not undermine divine trustworthiness; taklīf is merely a sign of reward or punishment.

Al-Razi begins by presenting the first objection from deniers of abrogation: if a prophet announces an eternal law that later changes, trust in all prophecies is lost. He offers two answers—first, exposing the opponent's contradiction, then the true answer that taklīf is merely an indication of reward or punishment, over which no one can object to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first objection carefully: it argues that if a prophet's earlier law could change, then Muhammad's claim of an eternal law is suspect, leading to loss of confidence in all laws.
2
Analyze the first answer
Note that al-Razi's first answer points out that the opponent's own stance (denying taklīf) is contradicted by his objection, which itself imposes a burden of belief.
3
Understand the true answer
The second answer is the core: taklīf is just an indication of divine reward or punishment, and God's assignment of these is not subject to human objection.
This is the 'true answer' of al-Razi's school.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation defends the possibility of abrogation without attributing imperfection to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون وأيضًا فلو جاز فيه أن يقال إنه إلا وإن أخبر أن شرعه دائم لكنه لم يدم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن محمدا له أخبر أن شرعه دائم، ثم إنه لا يدوم؟ ومعلوم أن [؟] عن جميع الشرائع، وهو باطل. هذا جائز. يوجب زوال الثقة.
الثاني : أنه تعالى لو أمر بشيء ثم نهى عنه لدل ذلك على البداء، وإنه غير.
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المصالح تتبدل بحسب اختلاف الأوقات، كالطبيب يأمر المريض بشرب شربة مخصوصة، ثم إنه ينهى عنه بعد ذلك؟ لأنا نقول: هذا إنما يقبل فيما يظهر في تبديله أثر وفائدة كما ذكرتم من تبديل العلاج بالعلاج وههنا ليس كذلك، فإن تحويل العبادة من السبت إلى الجمعة وتحويل القبلة من بيت المقدس إلى الكعبة لا ترى فيه فائدة ولا أثر البتة، فظهر الفرق.
الثالث : أن جملة اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها في البلاد ينقلون عن موسى أنه قال : " تمسكوا بالسبت ما دامت السماوات والأرض" وخبر التواتر مفيد للعلم، وقول موسى الحجة، ومتى ثبت هذان الأمران لزم الطعن في شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم
فهذا مجموع شبهات المنكرين.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجهين :
الأول : أن الخصم يقول : " القول بالتكليف باطل وهذا منه تكليف باعتقاد بطلان التكليف، فكان كلامه متناقضا.
الثاني - وهو الجواب الحقيقي - أن مذهبنا أن التكليف حاصله يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو أنه إعلام بنزول العقاب أو بنزول الثواب، فإن من صدر عنه الفعل الذي كلف به كان ذلك علامة على نزول الثواب ومن لم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل كان ذلك علامة على نزول العقاب وليس لأحد اعتراض على الله تعالى في أنه لِمَ خصص هذا بالثواب وذلك بالعقاب؟
وأما الشبهة الثانية فجوابها أن نقول : هب أن العقل كاف في التعريف، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون الفائدة في البعثة تأكيدًا لذاك التعريف؟ ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى من ذكر الدلائل على التوحيد مع أن الواحد منها كاف.
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها ظاهر على إنكار الحسن العقلي؛ لأنه تعالى يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وأما على تسليم الحسن والقبح العقليين فجوابها أن الحسن فيها على التفصيل، وإن لم يكن معلوما إلا أن وجه القبح فيها أيضا غير معلوم، ولا يبعد أن يحصل فيها وجه من وجوه الحكمة، وإن كنا لا نعرفه.
وأما الشبهة الرابعة فجوابها لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه ال بین أن شرعه منقطع مجذوذ، وكان معلومًا بالتواتر في دينه إلا أن قومه هلكوا بالكلية في زمان بخت نصر وصار الباقي أقل من عدد التواتر، فلا جرم انقطع هذا النقل. وقوله : النسخ يدل على البداء" قلنا : لا نسلم؛ فإنه لا يمتنع اختلاف الأحكام لاختلاف المصالح. وقوله : لا تأثير لتحويل العبادة من يوم السبت إلى يوم الجمعة" قلنا : كما لم يعلم وجه التفصيل في هذا التحويل لم يعلم أيضًا عدم الحكمة فيه، فسقط الاستدلال.
وقوله : اليهود نقلوا قوله تمسكوا بالسبت قلنا : إن تواترهم منقطع، فلا يفيد العلم، وبالله التوفيق.
The thirtieth issue. And also, if it is allowed to be said about it that it is not but [that he informed that his law is eternal but it did not last], then why is it not allowed to be said that Muhammad informed that his law is eternal, then it does not last? And it is known that [?] from all laws, and it is false. This is permissible. It necessitates loss of trust. Second: if He, exalted, commands something then forbids it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), and He is not [subject to that]. It is not said: why is it not allowed to say that interests (masālih) change according to different times, like a doctor ordering a patient to drink a specific draught then forbidding it later? Because we say: this is only accepted in what shows effect and benefit in its change, as you mentioned of changing treatment with treatment, and here it is not so; indeed, changing worship from Saturday to Friday and changing the qibla from Jerusalem to the Ka'ba, no benefit or effect is seen in it at all, so the difference is clear. Third: the entirety of the Jews, with their multitude and dispersal in the east and west of the earth in the lands, transmit from Moses that he said: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure,' and the mutawātir report yields knowledge, and the statement of Moses is proof, and when these two matters are established, it necessitates attacking the law of Muhammad, peace be upon him. So this is the totality of the objections of the deniers. The answer to the first objection is from two aspects: first: the opponent says 'the claim of taklīf (obligation) is false, and this [objection] is a taklīf of believing the falsehood of taklīf, so his speech is contradictory.' Second—and it is the true answer—that our school is that the essence of taklīf reduces to one thing, which is that it is an indication of the descent of punishment or the descent of reward; for whomever the act he was obliged to do issues from, that is a sign of the descent of reward, and whomever that act does not issue from, that is a sign of the descent of punishment, and no one has any objection to Allah Almighty regarding why He specified this with reward and that with punishment. As for the second objection, its answer is that we say: granted that intellect is sufficient for definition, but why is it not allowed that the benefit of sending messengers is an affirmation of that definition? For this reason Allah Almighty multiplied the evidences for tawḥīd (monotheism) even though one of them is sufficient. As for the third objection, its answer is apparent upon the denial of rational good (al-ḥusn al-'aqlī); because He, exalted, does what He wills and judges what He wills. But upon the admission of rational good and evil (al-qubh al-'aqlī), its answer is that the good in it is detailed, even if it is not known; except that the aspect of evil in it is also unknown, and it is not unlikely that there occurs in it an aspect of wisdom, even if we do not know it. As for the fourth objection, its answer: why is it not allowed to say that He clarified that His law is cut off and severed, and it was known through tawātur in His religion, except that his people perished entirely in the time of Nebuchadnezzar and the remainder became less than the number of tawātur, so inevitably this transmission was cut off. And his statement: 'abrogation indicates badā',' we say: we do not accept; indeed, it is not impossible that rulings differ due to differing interests. And his statement: 'there is no effect in changing worship from Saturday to Friday,' we say: just as the detailed wisdom in this change is not known, so too the absence of wisdom in it is not known, so the inference collapses. And his statement: 'the Jews transmitted his statement 'hold fast to the Sabbath,' we say: their tawātur is cut off, so it does not yield knowledge, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in which al-Razi presents four objections from deniers (al-munkirīn) against the abrogation of previous laws, then refutes each one. The objections concern: (1) the claim that Muhammad's announcement of an eternal law that did not last leads to loss of trust in all laws; (2) that God's commanding then forbidding implies badā' (change of mind) which is impossible for God; (3) that the Jews unanimously transmit Moses' command to keep the Sabbath forever, so abrogation would impugn Muhammad's prophethood. After listing these, al-Razi gives the answers. For the first objection, he offers two answers: first, that the opponent's own position is self-contradictory because he denies taklīf yet imposes a belief about it; second, the true answer—that taklīf is merely an indication of reward or punishment, and God may assign these as He wills without objection. For the second objection, he answers that even if reason could know, prophets confirm the rational knowledge. For the third, he answers that if one denies rational good and evil, God does as He wills; if one affirms them, the wisdom may be unknown but not necessarily absent. For the fourth objection, he addresses multiple sub-points: that the previous law's transmission was cut off when the people were destroyed; that abrogation does not imply badā' because rulings can change with interests; that the lack of perceived wisdom in changing worship does not prove absence of wisdom; and that the Jewish transmission is not reliably continuous. The passage concludes with 'and with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
النسخ (naskh)
التكليف (taklīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that abrogation implies God changed His mind (badā') or that laws must be eternal to be trustworthy.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that abrogation does not affect trust because taklīf is just a sign; God can change signs without changing His essence or purpose.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's true answer, what is the essence of taklīf?
A) An eternal decree that cannot be changed.
B) An indication of the descent of reward or punishment.
C) A rational obligation that must have a known benefit.
D) A test that God imposes arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against abrogation is refuted by showing that taklīf is merely a divine sign, not a permanent decree, so changing it does not undermine trust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a change in your life that feels like a contradiction of what you previously believed was settled, do you instinctively question God's wisdom, or trust that He is the only one who knows the full picture? What would it take for you to embrace uncertainty as a sign of His sovereignty rather than a threat to your security?
?
If taklīf is merely a sign, not a contract, then your obedience or disobedience is not about earning or losing something from God — it is about aligning yourself with His signs. Are you living as though your actions are transactions with God, or as responses to His unfolding guidance? Which mindset would free you from anxiety about reward and punishment?
