The Second Objection: Abrogation Implies Badā'?
Second objection and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that abrogation implies badā' (change of mind) and al-Razi's refutation that prophets serve to confirm rational knowledge.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not imply badā'; prophets merely confirm what reason already knows.

The second objection claims that if God commands something then later forbids it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), which is impossible for God. The denier preemptively rejects the analogy of a doctor changing a prescription, arguing that changes in worship like the Sabbath and qibla show no apparent benefit. Al-Razi answers that even if reason is sufficient for guidance, prophets serve to emphasize and confirm that rational knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The denier argues that God's change of command implies a change of mind (badā'), which is impossible. He then dismisses the doctor analogy because the changes in worship have no apparent benefit.
2
Analyze the doctor analogy
The denier says the doctor analogy fails because changing treatment has a visible effect, but changing the day of worship or qibla does not.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Razi replies that even if reason is enough, prophets are sent to reinforce the rational knowledge, just as Allah multiplies proofs for tawḥīd.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that abrogation is not a change in God's mind but a reaffirmation of the same rational truth in a new form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون وأيضًا فلو جاز فيه أن يقال إنه إلا وإن أخبر أن شرعه دائم لكنه لم يدم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن محمدا له أخبر أن شرعه دائم، ثم إنه لا يدوم؟ ومعلوم أن [؟] عن جميع الشرائع، وهو باطل. هذا جائز. يوجب زوال الثقة.
الثاني : أنه تعالى لو أمر بشيء ثم نهى عنه لدل ذلك على البداء، وإنه غير.
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المصالح تتبدل بحسب اختلاف الأوقات، كالطبيب يأمر المريض بشرب شربة مخصوصة، ثم إنه ينهى عنه بعد ذلك؟ لأنا نقول: هذا إنما يقبل فيما يظهر في تبديله أثر وفائدة كما ذكرتم من تبديل العلاج بالعلاج وههنا ليس كذلك، فإن تحويل العبادة من السبت إلى الجمعة وتحويل القبلة من بيت المقدس إلى الكعبة لا ترى فيه فائدة ولا أثر البتة، فظهر الفرق.
الثالث : أن جملة اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها في البلاد ينقلون عن موسى أنه قال : " تمسكوا بالسبت ما دامت السماوات والأرض" وخبر التواتر مفيد للعلم، وقول موسى الحجة، ومتى ثبت هذان الأمران لزم الطعن في شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم
فهذا مجموع شبهات المنكرين.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجهين :
الأول : أن الخصم يقول : " القول بالتكليف باطل وهذا منه تكليف باعتقاد بطلان التكليف، فكان كلامه متناقضا.
الثاني - وهو الجواب الحقيقي - أن مذهبنا أن التكليف حاصله يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو أنه إعلام بنزول العقاب أو بنزول الثواب، فإن من صدر عنه الفعل الذي كلف به كان ذلك علامة على نزول الثواب ومن لم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل كان ذلك علامة على نزول العقاب وليس لأحد اعتراض على الله تعالى في أنه لِمَ خصص هذا بالثواب وذلك بالعقاب؟
وأما الشبهة الثانية فجوابها أن نقول : هب أن العقل كاف في التعريف، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون الفائدة في البعثة تأكيدًا لذاك التعريف؟ ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى من ذكر الدلائل على التوحيد مع أن الواحد منها كاف.
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها ظاهر على إنكار الحسن العقلي؛ لأنه تعالى يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وأما على تسليم الحسن والقبح العقليين فجوابها أن الحسن فيها على التفصيل، وإن لم يكن معلوما إلا أن وجه القبح فيها أيضا غير معلوم، ولا يبعد أن يحصل فيها وجه من وجوه الحكمة، وإن كنا لا نعرفه.
وأما الشبهة الرابعة فجوابها لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه ال بین أن شرعه منقطع مجذوذ، وكان معلومًا بالتواتر في دينه إلا أن قومه هلكوا بالكلية في زمان بخت نصر وصار الباقي أقل من عدد التواتر، فلا جرم انقطع هذا النقل. وقوله : النسخ يدل على البداء" قلنا : لا نسلم؛ فإنه لا يمتنع اختلاف الأحكام لاختلاف المصالح. وقوله : لا تأثير لتحويل العبادة من يوم السبت إلى يوم الجمعة" قلنا : كما لم يعلم وجه التفصيل في هذا التحويل لم يعلم أيضًا عدم الحكمة فيه، فسقط الاستدلال.
وقوله : اليهود نقلوا قوله تمسكوا بالسبت قلنا : إن تواترهم منقطع، فلا يفيد العلم، وبالله التوفيق.
The thirtieth issue. And also, if it is allowed to be said about it that it is not but [that he informed that his law is eternal but it did not last], then why is it not allowed to be said that Muhammad informed that his law is eternal, then it does not last? And it is known that [?] from all laws, and it is false. This is permissible. It necessitates loss of trust. Second: if He, exalted, commands something then forbids it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), and He is not [subject to that]. It is not said: why is it not allowed to say that interests (masālih) change according to different times, like a doctor ordering a patient to drink a specific draught then forbidding it later? Because we say: this is only accepted in what shows effect and benefit in its change, as you mentioned of changing treatment with treatment, and here it is not so; indeed, changing worship from Saturday to Friday and changing the qibla from Jerusalem to the Ka'ba, no benefit or effect is seen in it at all, so the difference is clear. Third: the entirety of the Jews, with their multitude and dispersal in the east and west of the earth in the lands, transmit from Moses that he said: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure,' and the mutawātir report yields knowledge, and the statement of Moses is proof, and when these two matters are established, it necessitates attacking the law of Muhammad, peace be upon him. So this is the totality of the objections of the deniers. The answer to the first objection is from two aspects: first: the opponent says 'the claim of taklīf (obligation) is false, and this [objection] is a taklīf of believing the falsehood of taklīf, so his speech is contradictory.' Second—and it is the true answer—that our school is that the essence of taklīf reduces to one thing, which is that it is an indication of the descent of punishment or the descent of reward; for whomever the act he was obliged to do issues from, that is a sign of the descent of reward, and whomever that act does not issue from, that is a sign of the descent of punishment, and no one has any objection to Allah Almighty regarding why He specified this with reward and that with punishment. As for the second objection, its answer is that we say: granted that intellect is sufficient for definition, but why is it not allowed that the benefit of sending messengers is an affirmation of that definition? For this reason Allah Almighty multiplied the evidences for tawḥīd (monotheism) even though one of them is sufficient. As for the third objection, its answer is apparent upon the denial of rational good (al-ḥusn al-'aqlī); because He, exalted, does what He wills and judges what He wills. But upon the admission of rational good and evil (al-qubh al-'aqlī), its answer is that the good in it is detailed, even if it is not known; except that the aspect of evil in it is also unknown, and it is not unlikely that there occurs in it an aspect of wisdom, even if we do not know it. As for the fourth objection, its answer: why is it not allowed to say that He clarified that His law is cut off and severed, and it was known through tawātur in His religion, except that his people perished entirely in the time of Nebuchadnezzar and the remainder became less than the number of tawātur, so inevitably this transmission was cut off. And his statement: 'abrogation indicates badā',' we say: we do not accept; indeed, it is not impossible that rulings differ due to differing interests. And his statement: 'there is no effect in changing worship from Saturday to Friday,' we say: just as the detailed wisdom in this change is not known, so too the absence of wisdom in it is not known, so the inference collapses. And his statement: 'the Jews transmitted his statement 'hold fast to the Sabbath,' we say: their tawātur is cut off, so it does not yield knowledge, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in which al-Razi presents four objections from deniers (al-munkirīn) against the abrogation of previous laws, then refutes each one. The objections concern: (1) the claim that Muhammad's announcement of an eternal law that did not last leads to loss of trust in all laws; (2) that God's commanding then forbidding implies badā' (change of mind) which is impossible for God; (3) that the Jews unanimously transmit Moses' command to keep the Sabbath forever, so abrogation would impugn Muhammad's prophethood. After listing these, al-Razi gives the answers. For the first objection, he offers two answers: first, that the opponent's own position is self-contradictory because he denies taklīf yet imposes a belief about it; second, the true answer—that taklīf is merely an indication of reward or punishment, and God may assign these as He wills without objection. For the second objection, he answers that even if reason could know, prophets confirm the rational knowledge. For the third, he answers that if one denies rational good and evil, God does as He wills; if one affirms them, the wisdom may be unknown but not necessarily absent. For the fourth objection, he addresses multiple sub-points: that the previous law's transmission was cut off when the people were destroyed; that abrogation does not imply badā' because rulings can change with interests; that the lack of perceived wisdom in changing worship does not prove absence of wisdom; and that the Jewish transmission is not reliably continuous. The passage concludes with 'and with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
البداء (badā')
المصلحة (maṣlaḥah)
التكليف (taklīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that abrogation implies God changed His mind (badā').
Correction: Al-Razi shows that abrogation is like the multiplication of proofs—it reinforces the same message, not changes it.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer to the second objection, what is the benefit of sending prophets?
A) To introduce entirely new laws unknown to reason.
B) To confirm and emphasize what reason already knows.
C) To change the divine will according to changing interests.
D) To perform miracles that prove God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation does not indicate badā' because prophets confirm rational knowledge; change in law reinforces, not contradicts, the divine message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's wisdom when a command you were used to changed? How does knowing that abrogation only confirms already-known truths change your trust in His consistency?
?
If prophets are sent to confirm what reason already knows, why do you still turn away from that knowledge? What rational truth have you not yet fully embraced?
?
The denier thought change in law implies God's ignorance. Examine your own heart: when faced with a difficult ruling from the Shari'ah, do you unconsciously assume it is 'wrong' or 'outdated'? How will you combat that assumption today?

The Third Objection: Jewish Tawātur Against Abrogation
Third objection and its two-part answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection based on Jewish tawātur and al-Razi's answer that appeals to rational good/evil and unknown wisdom.

Core Idea
Jewish tawātur is not dispositive; wisdom may be hidden in abrogation.

The third objection argues that the Jews continuously transmit Moses' command to keep the Sabbath forever, and since tawātur yields certain knowledge, Muhammad's abrogation of it must be false. Al-Razi gives two answers: for those who deny rational good and evil, God does as He wills; for those who affirm them, the wisdom in abrogation may be unknown but not necessarily absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that all Jews transmit from Moses an eternal Sabbath command; tawātur provides certainty, so abrogation is impossible.
2
Understand the first answer
If one denies rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubh al-'aqlī), then God can do whatever He wills without restriction.
This is the Ash'ari position.
3
Understand the second answer
If one admits rational good and evil, the good in abrogation may be detailed but unknown, and the aspect of evil also unknown; it is possible there is wisdom we do not perceive.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Note how al-Razi provides answers for both positions, showing his comprehensive approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The thirtieth issue. And also, if it is allowed to be said about it that it is not but [that he informed that his law is eternal but it did not last], then why is it not allowed to be said that Muhammad informed that his law is eternal, then it does not last? And it is known that [?] from all laws, and it is false. This is permissible. It necessitates loss of trust. Second: if He, exalted, commands something then forbids it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), and He is not [subject to that]. It is not said: why is it not allowed to say that interests (masālih) change according to different times, like a doctor ordering a patient to drink a specific draught then forbidding it later? Because we say: this is only accepted in what shows effect and benefit in its change, as you mentioned of changing treatment with treatment, and here it is not so; indeed, changing worship from Saturday to Friday and changing the qibla from Jerusalem to the Ka'ba, no benefit or effect is seen in it at all, so the difference is clear. So this is the totality of the objections of the deniers. The answer to the first objection is from two aspects: first: the opponent says 'the claim of taklīf (obligation) is false, and this [objection] is a taklīf of believing the falsehood of taklīf, so his speech is contradictory.' Second—and it is the true answer—that our school is that the essence of taklīf reduces to one thing, which is that it is an indication of the descent of punishment or the descent of reward; for whomever the act he was obliged to do issues from, that is a sign of the descent of reward, and whomever that act does not issue from, that is a sign of the descent of punishment, and no one has any objection to Allah Almighty regarding why He specified this with reward and that with punishment. As for the second objection, its answer is that we say: granted that intellect is sufficient for definition, but why is it not allowed that the benefit of sending messengers is an affirmation of that definition? For this reason Allah Almighty multiplied the evidences for tawḥīd (monotheism) even though one of them is sufficient. As for the third objection, its answer is apparent upon the denial of rational good (al-ḥusn al-'aqlī); because He, exalted, does what He wills and judges what He wills. But upon the admission of rational good and evil (al-qubh al-'aqlī), its answer is that the good in it is detailed, even if it is not known; except that the aspect of evil in it is also unknown, and it is not unlikely that there occurs in it an aspect of wisdom, even if we do not know it. And his statement: 'abrogation indicates badā',' we say: we do not accept; indeed, it is not impossible that rulings differ due to differing interests. And his statement: 'there is no effect in changing worship from Saturday to Friday,' we say: just as the detailed wisdom in this change is not known, so too the absence of wisdom in it is not known, so the inference collapses. And his statement: 'the Jews transmitted his statement 'hold fast to the Sabbath,' we say: their tawātur is cut off, so it does not yield knowledge, and with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in which al-Razi presents four objections from deniers (al-munkirīn) against the abrogation of previous laws, then refutes each one. The objections concern: (1) the claim that Muhammad's announcement of an eternal law that did not last leads to loss of trust in all laws; (2) that God's commanding then forbidding implies badā' (change of mind) which is impossible for God; (3) that the Jews unanimously transmit Moses' command to keep the Sabbath forever, so abrogation would impugn Muhammad's prophethood. After listing these, al-Razi gives the answers. For the first objection, he offers two answers: first, that the opponent's own position is self-contradictory because he denies taklīf yet imposes a belief about it; second, the true answer—that taklīf is merely an indication of reward or punishment, and God may assign these as He wills without objection. For the second objection, he answers that even if reason could know, prophets confirm the rational knowledge. For the third, he answers that if one denies rational good and evil, God does as He wills; if one affirms them, the wisdom may be unknown but not necessarily absent. For the fourth objection, he addresses multiple sub-points: that the previous law's transmission was cut off when the people were destroyed; that abrogation does not imply badā' because rulings can change with interests; that the lack of perceived wisdom in changing worship does not prove absence of wisdom; and that the Jewish transmission is not reliably continuous. The passage concludes with 'and with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
التواتر (tawātur)
الحسن العقلي (al-ḥusn al-'aqlī)
القبح العقلي (al-qubh al-'aqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tawātur from Moses proves the Sabbath law is eternal and unchangeable.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if tawātur is accepted, it does not preclude abrogation because God's actions are not constrained by human perceptions of good and evil, and hidden wisdom may exist.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer for those who affirm rational good and evil, why might the abrogation of the Sabbath be acceptable?
A) Because the Sabbath law was never authentically transmitted.
B) Because the good in abrogation is known and clear.
C) Because the evil in abrogation is unknown, and there may be hidden wisdom.
D) Because God is not subject to any standard.

Key Takeaway
Jewish tawātur does not force the impossibility of abrogation; hidden wisdom and God's sovereignty over moral categories allow it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When God commands you to abandon something you hold dear or have been taught as permanent, do you trust His hidden wisdom, or do you demand an explanation before you obey? Which camp—denying or affirming rational good/evil—do you actually live by?
?
The objection relies on the strength of tradition (tawātur). Have you ever clung to a religious practice or belief because 'everyone does it' or 'it has always been this way', even when God's clear command seemed to point elsewhere? Are you ready to obey if abrogation comes in your own life?
?
Al-Razi argues that even if we don't see the wisdom in a divine decree, we must assume there is one. Think of a command from God that you struggle to understand. Will you let the tension drive you to doubt, or will you fall back on the certainty that His wisdom is greater than yours?

The Fourth Objection: Abrogation Cuts Off Transmission
Fourth objection and its multi-point refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection concerning the cut-off of transmission and al-Razi's multi-point refutation.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not imply badā', and lack of perceived wisdom does not prove its absence.

The fourth objection raises several points: that the previous law was known via tawātur to be eternal, but abrogation would cut off that transmission; that abrogation implies badā'; that the change of worship has no effect; and that Jewish transmission is reliable. Al-Razi addresses each: the transmission was cut off historically, abrogation does not imply badā' because rulings change with interests, the lack of perceived wisdom does not prove absence of wisdom, and Jewish tawātur is not continuous.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the entire passage which includes multiple sub-arguments and their refutations.
The objection is not explicitly stated but is inferred from the answer.
2
First point: cut-off of transmission
Al-Razi argues that the previous law's transmission ended when the people were destroyed in Nebuchadnezzar's time, so tawātur was broken.
3
Second point: abrogation and badā'
He denies that abrogation implies badā'; rulings can change with changing interests (maṣālih).
4
Third point: lack of perceived wisdom
Just because we don't see wisdom in changing worship doesn't mean it's absent; unknown wisdom may exist.
5
Fourth point: Jewish tawātur
Al-Razi says the Jewish transmission is actually cut off, so it does not yield certain knowledge.

المسألة الثلاثون وأيضًا فلو جاز فيه أن يقال إنه إلا وإن أخبر أن شرعه دائم لكنه لم يدم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن محمدا له أخبر أن شرعه دائم، ثم إنه لا يدوم؟ ومعلوم أن [؟] عن جميع الشرائع، وهو باطل. هذا جائز. يوجب زوال الثقة.
الثاني : أنه تعالى لو أمر بشيء ثم نهى عنه لدل ذلك على البداء، وإنه غير.
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المصالح تتبدل بحسب اختلاف الأوقات، كالطبيب يأمر المريض بشرب شربة مخصوصة، ثم إنه ينهى عنه بعد ذلك؟ لأنا نقول: هذا إنما يقبل فيما يظهر في تبديله أثر وفائدة كما ذكرتم من تبديل العلاج بالعلاج وههنا ليس كذلك، فإن تحويل العبادة من السبت إلى الجمعة وتحويل القبلة من بيت المقدس إلى الكعبة لا ترى فيه فائدة ولا أثر البتة، فظهر الفرق.
الثالث : أن جملة اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها في البلاد ينقلون عن موسى أنه قال : " تمسكوا بالسبت ما دامت السماوات والأرض" وخبر التواتر مفيد للعلم، وقول موسى الحجة، ومتى ثبت هذان الأمران لزم الطعن في شرع محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم
فهذا مجموع شبهات المنكرين.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى من وجهين :
الأول : أن الخصم يقول : " القول بالتكليف باطل وهذا منه تكليف باعتقاد بطلان التكليف، فكان كلامه متناقضا.
الثاني - وهو الجواب الحقيقي - أن مذهبنا أن التكليف حاصله يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو أنه إعلام بنزول العقاب أو بنزول الثواب، فإن من صدر عنه الفعل الذي كلف به كان ذلك علامة على نزول الثواب ومن لم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل كان ذلك علامة على نزول العقاب وليس لأحد اعتراض على الله تعالى في أنه لِمَ خصص هذا بالثواب وذلك بالعقاب؟
وأما الشبهة الثانية فجوابها أن نقول : هب أن العقل كاف في التعريف، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون الفائدة في البعثة تأكيدًا لذاك التعريف؟ ولهذا السبب أكثر الله تعالى من ذكر الدلائل على التوحيد مع أن الواحد منها كاف.
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فجوابها ظاهر على إنكار الحسن العقلي؛ لأنه تعالى يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وأما على تسليم الحسن والقبح العقليين فجوابها أن الحسن فيها على التفصيل، وإن لم يكن معلوما إلا أن وجه القبح فيها أيضا غير معلوم، ولا يبعد أن يحصل فيها وجه من وجوه الحكمة، وإن كنا لا نعرفه.
وأما الشبهة الرابعة فجوابها لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه ال بین أن شرعه منقطع مجذوذ، وكان معلومًا بالتواتر في دينه إلا أن قومه هلكوا بالكلية في زمان بخت نصر وصار الباقي أقل من عدد التواتر، فلا جرم انقطع هذا النقل. وقوله : النسخ يدل على البداء" قلنا : لا نسلم؛ فإنه لا يمتنع اختلاف الأحكام لاختلاف المصالح. وقوله : لا تأثير لتحويل العبادة من يوم السبت إلى يوم الجمعة" قلنا : كما لم يعلم وجه التفصيل في هذا التحويل لم يعلم أيضًا عدم الحكمة فيه، فسقط الاستدلال.
وقوله : اليهود نقلوا قوله تمسكوا بالسبت قلنا : إن تواترهم منقطع، فلا يفيد العلم، وبالله التوفيق.
The thirtieth issue. And also, if it is allowed to be said about it that it is not but [that he informed that his law is eternal but it did not last], then why is it not allowed to be said that Muhammad informed that his law is eternal, then it does not last? And it is known that [?] from all laws, and it is false. This is permissible. It necessitates loss of trust. Second: if He, exalted, commands something then forbids it, that would indicate badā' (change of mind), and He is not [subject to that]. It is not said: why is it not allowed to say that interests (masālih) change according to different times, like a doctor ordering a patient to drink a specific draught then forbidding it later? Because we say: this is only accepted in what shows effect and benefit in its change, as you mentioned of changing treatment with treatment, and here it is not so; indeed, changing worship from Saturday to Friday and changing the qibla from Jerusalem to the Ka'ba, no benefit or effect is seen in it at all, so the difference is clear. Third: the entirety of the Jews, with their multitude and dispersal in the east and west of the earth in the lands, transmit from Moses that he said: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure,' and the mutawātir report yields knowledge, and the statement of Moses is proof, and when these two matters are established, it necessitates attacking the law of Muhammad, peace be upon him. So this is the totality of the objections of the deniers. The answer to the first objection is from two aspects: first: the opponent says 'the claim of taklīf (obligation) is false, and this [objection] is a taklīf of believing the falsehood of taklīf, so his speech is contradictory.' Second—and it is the true answer—that our school is that the essence of taklīf reduces to one thing, which is that it is an indication of the descent of punishment or the descent of reward; for whomever the act he was obliged to do issues from, that is a sign of the descent of reward, and whomever that act does not issue from, that is a sign of the descent of punishment, and no one has any objection to Allah Almighty regarding why He specified this with reward and that with punishment. As for the second objection, its answer is that we say: granted that intellect is sufficient for definition, but why is it not allowed that the benefit of sending messengers is an affirmation of that definition? For this reason Allah Almighty multiplied the evidences for tawḥīd (monotheism) even though one of them is sufficient. As for the third objection, its answer is apparent upon the denial of rational good (al-ḥusn al-'aqlī); because He, exalted, does what He wills and judges what He wills. But upon the admission of rational good and evil (al-qubh al-'aqlī), its answer is that the good in it is detailed, even if it is not known; except that the aspect of evil in it is also unknown, and it is not unlikely that there occurs in it an aspect of wisdom, even if we do not know it. As for the fourth objection, its answer: why is it not allowed to say that He clarified that His law is cut off and severed, and it was known through tawātur in His religion, except that his people perished entirely in the time of Nebuchadnezzar and the remainder became less than the number of tawātur, so inevitably this transmission was cut off. And his statement: 'abrogation indicates badā',' we say: we do not accept; indeed, it is not impossible that rulings differ due to differing interests. And his statement: 'there is no effect in changing worship from Saturday to Friday,' we say: just as the detailed wisdom in this change is not known, so too the absence of wisdom in it is not known, so the inference collapses. And his statement: 'the Jews transmitted his statement 'hold fast to the Sabbath,' we say: their tawātur is cut off, so it does not yield knowledge, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in which al-Razi presents four objections from deniers (al-munkirīn) against the abrogation of previous laws, then refutes each one. The objections concern: (1) the claim that Muhammad's announcement of an eternal law that did not last leads to loss of trust in all laws; (2) that God's commanding then forbidding implies badā' (change of mind) which is impossible for God; (3) that the Jews unanimously transmit Moses' command to keep the Sabbath forever, so abrogation would impugn Muhammad's prophethood. After listing these, al-Razi gives the answers. For the first objection, he offers two answers: first, that the opponent's own position is self-contradictory because he denies taklīf yet imposes a belief about it; second, the true answer—that taklīf is merely an indication of reward or punishment, and God may assign these as He wills without objection. For the second objection, he answers that even if reason could know, prophets confirm the rational knowledge. For the third, he answers that if one denies rational good and evil, God does as He wills; if one affirms them, the wisdom may be unknown but not necessarily absent. For the fourth objection, he addresses multiple sub-points: that the previous law's transmission was cut off when the people were destroyed; that abrogation does not imply badā' because rulings can change with interests; that the lack of perceived wisdom in changing worship does not prove absence of wisdom; and that the Jewish transmission is not reliably continuous. The passage concludes with 'and with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
التواتر (tawātur)
البداء (badā')
المصلحة (maṣlaḥah)
الحكمة (ḥikmah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Jewish transmission of the Sabbath law is unbroken and universally accepted.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that their tawātur was cut off historically, so it does not produce certainty.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is the Jewish transmission of the Sabbath command not reliable?
A) Because the Jews are not trustworthy.
B) Because their tawātur was cut off after Nebuchadnezzar's destruction.
C) Because Moses never said that.
D) Because Muhammad abrogated it.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because transmission was broken, abrogation does not imply badā', hidden wisdom may exist, and Jewish tawātur is not continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command in the Quran that you do not understand the wisdom behind, do you trust that Allah has a reason, or do you secretly doubt? What specific daily practice can you adopt to strengthen your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
The Jews clung to the Sabbath because they believed their tradition was unbroken. Is there any tradition or practice you hold onto simply because it feels ancient, without seeking its guidance from the Quran and Sunnah? What step will you take this week to re-examine one such practice?
?
Abrogation does not mean God changes His mind. Have you ever felt that a change in your life situation meant that Allah's plan had changed? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of divine decree and your own constancy in worship?

The Four Areas of Disagreement on Prophetic Infallibility
Razi enumerates the four topics of disagreement on prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four areas in which Muslims have disagreed regarding the infallibility of the prophets, including the consensus and exceptions.

Core Idea
The infallibility of prophets is agreed upon in creed and transmission, but disputed in fatwa and actions.

Imam al-Razi begins his treatment of prophetic infallibility by mapping out the terrain of disagreement. He identifies four distinct areas: belief, delivery of divine laws, fatwa, and actions. In this lesson, we examine the first three areas, where there is either consensus or limited dissent, and see how the fourth area opens the door to multiple schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the four areas
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let Razi's structure come through.
Notice how each area is introduced with 'Ajam'at al-umma' or 'Ikhtalafat'.
2
Identify the first three areas and their consensus
The first three areas (belief, tabligh, fatwa) are each introduced with a statement of consensus. Note the exceptions for belief (Fadliyya, Rawafid) and the disagreement on inadvertent error in fatwa.
The consensus on fatwa is only on deliberate error; inadvertent error is disputed.
3
Understand the fourth area as the most disputed
The fourth area (actions and states) is introduced as one on which the community has differed into five schools. This sets up the next lesson.
The phrase 'ikhtalafat fi salam' signals the main point of difference.
4
Reflect on the significance of these areas
Why did Razi start with these four areas? Think about how they cover the full scope of prophetic roles: belief, revelation, legal authority, and personal conduct.
This taxonomy is a common method in kalam to organize discussion.

المسألة الثانية والثلاثون
في عصمة الأنبياء صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم
اعلم أن الاختلاف في هذه المسألة واقع في أربعة مواضع:
الأول : ما يتعلق بالاعتقاد وأجمعت الأمة على أنهم معصومون عن الكفر والبدعة، إلا الفضلية من الخوارج فإنهم يجوزون الكفر على الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - وذلك لأن عندهم يجوز صدور الذنب عنهم، وكل ذنب فهو كفر عندهم، وبهذا الطريق جوزوا صدور الكفر عنهم، وأما الروافض فإنهم يجوزون عليهم إظهار كلمة الكفر على سبيل التقية.
الثاني : : ما يتعلق بتبليغ الشرائع والأحكام من الله تعالى : وأجمعت الأمة على أنه لا يجوز عليهم التحريف والخيانة في هذا الباب لا بالعمد ولا بالسهو، وإلا لم يبق الاعتماد على شيء من الشرائع.
الثالث : ما يتعلق بالفتوى وأجمعت الأمة على أنه لا يجوز تعمد الخطأ، أما
على سبيل السهو فقد اختلفوا فيه.
الرابع : ما يتعلق بأفعالهم وأحوالهم وقد اختلفت فيه الأمة على خمسة
مذاهب :
الأول قول الحشوية وهو أنه يجوز عليهم الإقدام على الكبائر والصغائر.
،
الثاني : : أنه لا يجوز منهم تعمد الكبيرة ،البتة وأما تعمد الصغيرة فهو جائز بشرط أن لا يكون منفرًا، فأما إن كان تعمد الصغيرة منفرًا فذلك لا يجوز عليهم،، مثل التطفيف بما دون الحبة وهذا قول أكثر المعتزلة.
الثالث : أنه لا يجوز عليهم تعمد الكبيرة والصغيرة ولكن يجوز صدور الذنب
منهم على سبيل الخطأ في التأويل، وهذا قول الجبائي. الرابع : : أنه لا يجوز الكبيرة ولا الصغيرة لا تعمدًا ولا بالتأويل الخطأ، أما
The Thirty-Second Question: On the Infallibility of the Prophets, may God's blessings and peace be upon them. Know that disagreement in this matter occurs in four areas: First: What pertains to belief. The community is agreed that they are protected from disbelief and innovation, except for the Fadliyya of the Khawarij who permit disbelief upon the prophets — may God's blessings and peace be upon them — because according to them sin may issue from them, and every sin is disbelief according to them, and by this way they permitted disbelief from them. As for the Rawafid, they permit them to show the word of disbelief by way of taqiyya. Second: What pertains to the delivery of the laws and rulings from God Most High. The community is agreed that it is not permissible for them to distort or betray in this matter, neither deliberately nor inadvertently, otherwise no reliance would remain on any of the laws. Third: What pertains to fatwa. The community is agreed that deliberate error is not permissible; as for inadvertent error, they have differed on it. Fourth: What pertains to their actions and states. The community has differed on this into five schools: The first: the saying of the Hashwiyya, that it is permissible for them to commit major and minor sins. The second: that deliberate major sin is absolutely not permissible from them; as for deliberate minor sin, it is permissible on condition that it is not repulsive. But if deliberate minor sin is repulsive, then it is not permissible for them — like cheating in weights concerning less than a grain — and this is the saying of most Mu'tazila. The third: that deliberate major and minor sin are not permissible from them, but the commission of sin is permissible by way of error in interpretation, and this is the saying of al-Jubba'i. The fourth: that major and minor sin are not permissible, neither deliberately nor by erroneous interpretation. As for...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the disagreements regarding the infallibility ('isma) of the prophets. Razi introduces the thirty-second question and states that the disagreement occurs in four areas (mawādi'). The first area is belief (i'tiqād): the community (umma) agrees that prophets are protected from kufr (disbelief) and bid'a (innovation), except for the Fadliyya of the Khawarij, who allow kufr because for them every sin is kufr, and the Rawafid (Shi'a), who allow outward display of kufr under taqiyya (dissimulation). The second area is the delivery (tablīgh) of divine laws and rulings: the umma agrees that prophets cannot deliberately or inadvertently distort or betray, otherwise no certainty remains in the laws. The third area is fatwa (issuing legal opinions): the umma agrees that deliberate error is not permitted, but they differ on inadvertent error. The fourth area is their actions and states (af'āl wa aḥwāl): the umma has differed into five schools (madhāhib). Razi then lists the first four schools: 1) The Hashwiyya hold that prophets may commit both major and minor sins. 2) Most Mu'tazila hold that deliberate major sin is absolutely not permitted, but deliberate minor sin is permitted if it is not repulsive (e.g., cheating in weights below a grain); if repulsive, it is not permitted. 3) al-Jubba'i holds that deliberate major and minor sins are not permitted, but sin may occur by error in interpretation (ta'wil). 4) The fourth school, which appears to be the preferred view, holds that neither major nor minor sins occur from prophets, neither deliberately nor by erroneous interpretation. The text is cut off before the fifth school is described.

Key Terms
عصمة الأنبياء
الخوارج
الروافض
التقية
السهو
الفتوى
تبليغ الشرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree that prophets are infallible in creed and cannot show disbelief in any way.
Correction: The Fadliyya (Khawarij) allow Kufr from prophets because every sin is Kufr in their view, and the Rawafid (Shi'a) allow outward Kufr under taqiyya. Consensus is not absolute.

Checkpoint
On which area of prophetic infallibility is there complete agreement among the umma?
A) Belief
B) Delivery of the laws and rulings
C) Fatwa
D) Actions and states

Key Takeaway
The four areas of disagreement on prophetic infallibility are belief, delivery of revelation, fatwa, and actions, with the first two having consensus (with exceptions) and the fourth being the most disputed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that some groups allowed prophets to commit disbelief under taqiyya (dissimulation). When have you hidden or compromised your faith out of fear of others? How does the prophetic example of unwavering truthfulness in delivering revelation confront that tendency in your own heart? What one step will you take this week to be more open about your beliefs, even at personal cost?
?
Razi mentions that deliberate error in fatwa is unanimously forbidden, but inadvertent error is disputed. This reminds us that even scholars can be wrong about secondary matters. In what area of your life are you most resistant to admitting you might be mistaken? Are you willing to seek correction from someone you trust? Commit to asking one person this week for honest feedback on a belief or habit you hold dear.
?
The first area of infallibility (belief) has exceptions from the Fadliyya and Rawafid. This forces you to consider: Do you truly believe that the Prophet ﷺ was completely free from any innovation or disbelief in his beliefs? Or do you harbor a subtle suspicion that he might have had a moment of doubt? Examine your heart—are you fully convinced of his absolute purity in faith, and if not, what is holding you back from complete trust?

The Schools on Prophetic Actions: Four Views (Incomplete)
Razi lists four of the five schools on prophetic actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four schools of thought on whether prophets can commit sins in their actions, as listed by Imam al-Razi, and note that the fifth school is missing from the text.

Core Idea
Scholars differ from allowing all sins to forbidding all sins, with intermediate positions on minor sins and error in interpretation.

In the fourth area of prophetic infallibility—their actions and states—Imam al-Razi lists five distinct schools. Unfortunately, the text cuts off after the fourth school. In this lesson, we cover the views of the Hashwiyya, most Mu'tazila, al-Jubba'i, and a fourth school, noting that the fifth is lost.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the four schools
Read the Arabic and translation for the four schools. Pay attention to the nuance in each position.
The text has some punctuation irregularities; focus on the content.
2
Identify the four schools and their positions
List each school: Hashwiyya (permit both kabira and saghira), Mu'tazila (forbid kabira, permit saghira if not munaffir), Jubba'i (forbid 'amdan, permit by khata' fi ta'wil), fourth (forbid all).
3
Understand the reasoning behind each position
For the Mu'tazila, the condition 'not repulsive' is key; al-Jubba'i invokes error in interpretation; the fourth school is stricter.
The fourth school does not allow ta'wil error either.
4
Reflect on the missing fifth school
The text ends abruptly at 'amma'. Consider what the fifth school might be based on the patterns of the first four.
It likely completes the spectrum or offers a different criterion.

The Thirty-Second Question: On the Infallibility of the Prophets, may God's blessings and peace be upon them. Know that disagreement in this matter occurs in four areas: First: What pertains to belief. The community is agreed that they are protected from disbelief and innovation, except for the Fadliyya of the Khawarij who permit disbelief upon the prophets — may God's blessings and peace be upon them — because according to them sin may issue from them, and every sin is disbelief according to them, and by this way they permitted disbelief from them. As for the Rawafid, they permit them to show the word of disbelief by way of taqiyya. Second: What pertains to the delivery of the laws and rulings from God Most High. The community is agreed that it is not permissible for them to distort or betray in this matter, neither deliberately nor inadvertently, otherwise no reliance would remain on any of the laws. Third: What pertains to fatwa. The community is agreed that deliberate error is not permissible; as for inadvertent error, they have differed on it. Fourth: What pertains to their actions and states. The community has differed on this into five schools: The first: the saying of the Hashwiyya, that it is permissible for them to commit major and minor sins. The second: that deliberate major sin is absolutely not permissible from them; as for deliberate minor sin, it is permissible on condition that it is not repulsive. But if deliberate minor sin is repulsive, then it is not permissible for them — like cheating in weights concerning less than a grain — and this is the saying of most Mu'tazila. The third: that deliberate major and minor sin are not permissible from them, but the commission of sin is permissible by way of error in interpretation, and this is the saying of al-Jubba'i. The fourth: that major and minor sin are not permissible, neither deliberately nor by erroneous interpretation. As for...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the disagreements regarding the infallibility ('isma) of the prophets. Razi introduces the thirty-second question and states that the disagreement occurs in four areas (mawādi'). The first area is belief (i'tiqād): the community (umma) agrees that prophets are protected from kufr (disbelief) and bid'a (innovation), except for the Fadliyya of the Khawarij, who allow kufr because for them every sin is kufr, and the Rawafid (Shi'a), who allow outward display of kufr under taqiyya (dissimulation). The second area is the delivery (tablīgh) of divine laws and rulings: the umma agrees that prophets cannot deliberately or inadvertently distort or betray, otherwise no certainty remains in the laws. The third area is fatwa (issuing legal opinions): the umma agrees that deliberate error is not permitted, but they differ on inadvertent error. The fourth area is their actions and states (af'āl wa aḥwāl): the umma has differed into five schools (madhāhib). Razi then lists the first four schools: 1) The Hashwiyya hold that prophets may commit both major and minor sins. 2) Most Mu'tazila hold that deliberate major sin is absolutely not permitted, but deliberate minor sin is permitted if it is not repulsive (e.g., cheating in weights below a grain); if repulsive, it is not permitted. 3) al-Jubba'i holds that deliberate major and minor sins are not permitted, but sin may occur by error in interpretation (ta'wil). 4) The fourth school, which appears to be the preferred view, holds that neither major nor minor sins occur from prophets, neither deliberately nor by erroneous interpretation. The text is cut off before the fifth school is described.

Key Terms
الحشوية
المعتزلة
الجبائي
الصغيرة
الكبيرة
التأويل
التطفيف
المنفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila allow all minor sins from prophets.
Correction: The Mu'tazila only allow deliberate minor sins that are not repulsive (like cheating below a grain), and they forbid repulsive minor sins altogether.

Checkpoint
Which school allows prophets to commit major sins?
A) Hashwiyya
B) Most Mu'tazila
C) Al-Jubba'i
D) The fourth school

Key Takeaway
The first four schools on prophetic actions range from allowing all sins (Hashwiyya) to forbidding them completely (fourth school), with intermediate positions on minor sins and error in interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four views do you find most compelling, and what does your choice reveal about how you view the prophets' relationship with Allah? Are you comfortable with any level of imperfection in those who deliver Allah's message?
?
Al-Jubba'i allows sin by error in interpretation. Have you ever excused your own wrong actions by claiming you 'didn't understand' or 'misinterpreted' a situation? How does that compare to the standard you hold for prophets?
?
The Hashwiyya claim that prophets can commit major sins. If you heard someone today say that about a beloved prophet, how would it make you feel? Does that reaction come from your faith or your personal attachment? How can you ground your reverence in scripture rather than emotion?

First Area: Infallibility in Belief
Razi states the consensus on infallibility in belief and cites two sectarian exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first area of disagreement concerning prophetic infallibility—belief—and the exceptions raised by the Khawarij and Rafidites.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible in belief, but two sects dissent with extreme views.

Razi begins by outlining four areas of debate on prophetic infallibility. The first area concerns belief: the consensus is that prophets are protected from disbelief and innovation, yet two sects—the Fudaliyya Khawarij and the Rafidites—hold deviant views. Let's examine these exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice how Razi states the consensus and then presents the exceptions.
The passage uses 'إلا' to introduce exceptions.
2
Identify the consensus and exceptions
Find the consensus (أجمعت الأمة) and the two exceptional groups. Understand their reasoning as given by Razi.
The Khawarij consider every sin as disbelief; the Rafidites permit outward denial under duress.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these exceptions matter for the doctrine of prophetic infallibility. How do they contrast with the mainstream view?
This area shows that even basic beliefs about prophets have been contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية والثلاثون في عصمة الأنبياء صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم اعلم أن الاختلاف في هذه المسألة واقع في أربعة مواضع: الأول : ما يتعلق بالاعتقاد وأجمعت الأمة على أنهم معصومون عن الكفر والبدعة، إلا الفضلية من الخوارج فإنهم يجوزون الكفر على الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - وذلك لأن عندهم يجوز صدور الذنب عنهم، وكل ذنب فهو كفر عندهم، وبهذا الطريق جوزوا صدور الكفر عنهم، وأما الروافض فإنهم يجوزون عليهم إظهار كلمة الكفر على سبيل التقية. الثاني : ما يتعلق بتبليغ الشرائع والأحكام من الله تعالى : وأجمعت الأمة على أنه لا يجوز عليهم التحريف والخيانة في هذا الباب لا بالعمد ولا بالسهو، وإلا لم يبق الاعتماد على شيء من الشرائع.
The Second and Thirty-First Issue: On the Infallibility of the Prophets, may God's blessings and peace be upon them. Know that disagreement concerning this issue occurs in four areas: First: What pertains to belief. The community has agreed that they are infallible from disbelief and innovation, except for the Fudaliyya among the Khawarij, for they permit disbelief upon the prophets—may God's blessings and peace be upon them—because in their view, the commission of sin is permissible from them, and every sin in their view is disbelief, and through this way they permit the occurrence of disbelief from them. As for the Rafidites, they permit them to display the word of disbelief for the sake of dissimulation. Second: What pertains to conveying the divine laws and rulings from God Almighty. The community has agreed that it is not permissible for them to commit distortion or betrayal in this matter, neither intentionally nor by forgetfulness, otherwise trust would not remain in any of the divine laws.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage classifies the first two areas of disagreement concerning prophetic infallibility. Razi begins by stating that the issue of infallibility is debated in four areas. The first area is belief: the community agrees that prophets are infallible from disbelief and innovation, except the Fudaliyya sect of the Khawarij, who permit prophets to commit disbelief because they believe any sin is disbelief. The Rafidites also permit prophets to outwardly utter disbelief under dissimulation. The second area is conveying divine laws: here there is consensus that prophets cannot distort or betray God's message, whether intentionally or by forgetfulness, as that would undermine all revealed law.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree that prophets are completely infallible in belief without any exceptions.
Correction: While the majority holds this view, the Fudaliyya Khawarij and Rafidites present differing opinions as detailed by Razi.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are infallible in belief according to mainstream consensus, but the Fudaliyya Khawarij and Rafidites hold dissenting views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Rafidites allow outward denial under duress. Have you ever been in a situation where you were tempted to hide your beliefs for fear of criticism or loss? How does this exception make you examine your own commitment to truth?
?
The Khawarij equated every sin with disbelief. Do you ever find yourself judging others' sins as if they negate their entire faith? How does this passage call you to examine the severity of your judgments?

Second Area: Infallibility in Conveying Divine Laws
Razi affirms the consensus that prophets are immune from distortion in conveying divine laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second area of disagreement—conveying divine laws—and the consensus that prophets cannot distort or betray the message.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible in delivering God's laws; distortion would destroy all trust in revelation.

After discussing belief, Razi turns to the second area: the transmission of divine laws. Here there is unanimity that prophets cannot intentionally or unintentionally alter the message. This lesson examines that consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the second area. Notice how Razi presents the consensus and its rationale.
The passage is concise but carries a strong logical consequence.
2
Identify the consensus and its scope
The consensus covers both intentional and unintentional distortion. Why does Razi mention both?
Prophetic mission requires complete trustworthiness without any possibility of error.
3
Analyze the consequence
Razi says if distortion were possible, no trust would remain in any divine law. Reflect on why this is a necessary conclusion.
This shows the foundational importance of this infallibility.

Third and Fourth Areas: Fatwa and Actions with Five Schools
Razi enumerates the third and fourth areas with five schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five schools of thought on the possibility of sins for prophets in their actions and the rule on intentional error in fatwa.

Core Idea
Prophets cannot intentionally err in fatwa; actions are debated across five schools.

Razi continues the discussion of prophetic infallibility by examining the remaining two areas: fatwa and actions. While there is consensus that a prophet cannot intentionally err in giving a legal ruling, the issue of forgetfulness is disputed. Regarding actions, the community splits into five distinct positions, ranging from allowing all sins to prohibiting all sins absolutely. This lesson covers these positions in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fatwa and actions
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the third area (fatwa) and then the five schools listed for the fourth area. Note the consensus on intentional error and the disagreement on forgetfulness.
The passage is dense with technical terms like الكبيرة and الصغيرة.
2
Identify the five schools and their positions
List each school and what it allows or forbids. Pay attention to the conditions like 'repulsiveness' or 'erroneous interpretation'.
The schools are: Hashwiyya, most Mu'tazila, al-Jubba'i, a fourth unnamed group, and Rafidites.
3
Compare the schools on scope of permissibility
Notice the progression from absolute permission to absolute prohibition. Consider how each school understands the nature of prophetic responsibility.
The fourth school uniquely allows forgetfulness but rebukes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ما يتعلق بالفتوى وأجمعت الأمة على أنه لا يجوز تعمد الخطأ، أما على سبيل السهو فقد اختلفوا فيه. الرابع : ما يتعلق بأفعالهم وأحوالهم وقد اختلفت فيه الأمة على خمسة مذاهب : الأول قول الحشوية وهو أنه يجوز عليهم الإقدام على الكبائر والصغائر. الثاني : أنه لا يجوز منهم تعمد الكبيرة ،البتة وأما تعمد الصغيرة فهو جائز بشرط أن لا يكون منفرًا، فأما إن كان تعمد الصغيرة منفرًا فذلك لا يجوز عليهم، مثل التطفيف بما دون الحبة وهذا قول أكثر المعتزلة. الثالث : أنه لا يجوز عليهم تعمد الكبيرة والصغيرة ولكن يجوز صدور الذنب منهم على سبيل الخطأ في التأويل، وهذا قول الجبائي. الرابع : أنه لا يجوز الكبيرة ولا الصغيرة لا تعمدًا ولا بالتأويل الخطأ، أما السهو والنسيان فجائزان عليهم، ثم إنهم يعاتبون على ذلك السهو والنسيان، لما أن علومهم أكمل، فكان الواجب عليهم المبالغة في الصدق والتحفظ والتيقظ. الخامس : أن لا يجوز عليهم الصغيرة ولا الكبيرة لا بالعمد ولا بالتأويل ولا بالسهو ولا بالنسيان وهذا مذهب الروافض واختلفت الأمة أيضًا في وقت وجوب هذه العصمة فالروافض قالت: إنها من أول الولادة إلى آخر العمر، وقال الأكثرون : هذه العصمة إنما تجب في زمان النبوة، وأما قبل النبوة فهي غير واجبة، وهو قول أكثر أصحابنا، وقول أبي الهذيل وأبي علي الجبائي.
Third: What pertains to fatwa. The community has agreed that intentional error is not permissible, but concerning forgetfulness, they have differed. Fourth: What pertains to their actions and states. The community has differed concerning it into five schools: First: The statement of the Hashwiyya—that committing major and minor sins is permissible for them. Second: That deliberate commission of a major sin is absolutely not permissible for them, but deliberate commission of a minor sin is permissible on condition that it does not cause repulsion; if deliberate commission of a minor sin is repulsive, then that is not permissible for them, like fraud in less than a grain—this is the view of most Mu'tazila. Third: That deliberate commission of a major or minor sin is not permissible for them, but the occurrence of sin from them by way of error in interpretation is permissible—this is the view of al-Jubba'i. Fourth: That major and minor sins are not permissible, neither deliberately nor through erroneous interpretation, but forgetfulness and oversight are permissible for them; then they are rebuked for that forgetfulness and oversight because their knowledge is more complete, so it was incumbent upon them to be extreme in truthfulness, caution, and vigilance. Fifth: That minor nor major sins are not permissible for them, neither deliberately nor through interpretation, nor by forgetfulness nor oversight—this is the school of the Rafidites. The community also differed concerning the time of the obligation of this infallibility: the Rafidites said it is from the beginning of birth to the end of life, and the majority said: this infallibility is only obligatory during the time of prophethood, but before prophethood it is not obligatory—this is the view of most of our companions, and the view of Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of the four areas, covering the third and fourth areas and then the timing of infallibility. The third area is fatwa (legal ruling): the community agrees a prophet cannot intentionally err in a fatwa, but differs on forgetfulness. The fourth area concerns actions and states, where Razi lists five schools: (1) Hashwiyya allow both major and minor sins; (2) most Mu'tazila forbid deliberate major sins but allow deliberate minor sins unless they are repulsive; (3) al-Jubba'i forbids deliberate major and minor sins but permits sins from erroneous interpretation; (4) another view forbids both intentionally and via interpretation but allows forgetfulness, with rebuke due to their higher knowledge; (5) the Rafidites absolutely forbid all sins in any way. Razi then adds that the community also differed on when infallibility applies: the Rafidites say from birth to death; the majority (including most Ash'aris, Abu al-Hudhayl, and Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i) say it is only obligatory during prophethood.

الكبيرة
الصغيرة
الحشوية
المعتزلة
الجبائي
الرافضة

A) Deliberate minor sins
B) Sin via erroneous interpretation
C) Forgetfulness and oversight
D) All sins absolutely

Key Takeaway
The five schools on prophetic actions range from allowing all sins to prohibiting them entirely, with intermediate positions on minor sins, interpretation errors, and forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The fourth school rebukes prophets for forgetfulness because their knowledge is more complete. How does this heightened accountability challenge you in your own life—where might you be excusing your own negligence because 'I didn't know'?
?
If you believed, like the Rafidites, that prophets cannot have even forgetfulness, how would that affect your trust in their human experience? Conversely, if a prophet could sin through interpretation error, how would that impact your reliance on their rulings? Answer honestly: which view makes you feel more or less connected to the Prophet's humanity?

Timing of Infallibility: Before or During Prophethood?
Razi explains two views on the timing of infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on whether prophetic infallibility applies before prophethood or only during it.

Core Idea
Infallibility is only required during prophethood, not before, according to the majority.

After discussing the areas of infallibility, Razi turns to a related question: when does this infallibility begin? Is a prophet protected from sin even before receiving revelation? The community differs, with the Rafidites arguing for lifelong infallibility and the majority (including most Ash'aris and some Mu'tazila) restricting it to the prophetic period. This lesson examines these two views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on timing
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It presents two views on the timing of infallibility.
Pay attention to the terms 'قبل النبوة' (before prophethood) and 'زمان النبوة' (time of prophethood).
2
Identify the two opposing views
List the Rafidite view (from birth to death) and the majority view (only during prophethood). Note who is included in the majority.
The majority includes 'most of our companions' (Ash'aris) and Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the majority might restrict infallibility to prophethood. The passage does not give reasons, but it implies that before prophethood, the prophet is not divinely protected from sin.
This does not mean prophets sinned before prophethood, only that it was not obligatory for them to be infallible.

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of the four areas, covering the third and fourth areas and then the timing of infallibility. The third area is fatwa (legal ruling): the community agrees a prophet cannot intentionally err in a fatwa, but differs on forgetfulness. The fourth area concerns actions and states, where Razi lists five schools: (1) Hashwiyya allow both major and minor sins; (2) most Mu'tazila forbid deliberate major sins but allow deliberate minor sins unless they are repulsive; (3) al-Jubba'i forbids deliberate major and minor sins but permits sins from erroneous interpretation; (4) another view forbids both intentionally and via interpretation but allows forgetfulness, with rebuke due to their higher knowledge; (5) the Rafidites absolutely forbid all sins in any way. Razi then adds that the community also differed on when infallibility applies: the Rafidites say from birth to death; the majority (including most Ash'aris, Abu al-Hudhayl, and Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i) say it is only obligatory during prophethood.

Key Terms
عصمة
وقت وجوب
الروافض
قبل النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that prophets are infallible from birth.
Correction: The majority holds that infallibility is only required during prophethood, not before.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, when is infallibility obligatory?
A) From birth to death
B) Only during prophethood
C) Only after prophethood
D) At all times

Key Takeaway
The majority of theologians hold that prophets are infallible only during their prophetic mission, not before.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If prophets were not infallible before their mission, what does that say about God's ability to choose and purify whom He wills despite their past? Is there a past sin you think disqualifies you from being used by God? How does this lesson challenge that thought?
?
The Rafidites believe in infallibility from birth, while the majority limits it to prophethood. Which view makes you more aware of your own need for God's mercy and guidance in your current state? How does that awareness change how you approach your daily struggles with sin?
?
Razi states that infallibility is only 'obligatory' during prophethood. If God does not require sinlessness from you before He calls you to a task, what excuse do you have for not stepping up to serve Him because of your past mistakes? What step will you take this week to act despite your imperfections?

Razi's View on Prophetic Infallibility
Razi's definition of prophetic infallibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's specific position that prophets are infallible from intentional major and minor sins, but forgetfulness is permissible.

Core Idea
Infallibility covers intentional sins only; forgetfulness is not a sin.

Imam Razi opens this section by clearly stating his own view on prophetic infallibility. He distinguishes between intentional sins and those committed by forgetfulness. This distinction is crucial because it sets the scope of infallibility. Let's read his statement carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic statement
Read Razi's statement in Arabic to appreciate its precision. Note the key terms 'الكبائر' (major sins) and 'الصغائر' (minor sins).
Razi uses the phrase 'في زمان النبوة' (during the time of prophethood) to limit the scope.
2
Understand the distinction
Razi says prophets are infallible from intentional sins ('بالعمد') but not from forgetfulness ('على سبيل السهو'). Focus on why forgetfulness is treated differently.
Forgetfulness is not a deliberate act, so it does not contradict the purpose of prophethood.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider the difference between this view and other scholarly opinions. Razi's view is moderate, avoiding extremes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي نقوله : إن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - معصومون في زمان النبوة من الكبائر والصغائر بالعمد أما على سبيل السهو فجائز، ويدل على وجوب العصمة وجوه : الحجة الأولى : لو صدر الذنب عنهم لكان حالهم في استحقاق الذم عاجلا والعقاب آجلا أشد من حال عصاة الأمة، وهذا باطل، فصدور الذنب عنهم أيضا باطل بيان الملازمة أن أعظم نعم الله على العباد عطاؤهم نعمة الرسالة والنبوة، وكل من كانت نعم الله تعالى عليه أكثر كان صدور الذنب عنه أفحش، وصريح العقل يدل عليه، ثم يؤكده من النقل وجوه. أحدها : قوله : ينسَاءَ النَّبِيِّ لَسْتُنَّ كَأَحَدٍ مِّنَ النِّسَاء إِنِ اتَّقَيْتُ) [الأحزاب: ٣٢]، هو مَن يَأْتِ مِنكُنَ بِفَاحِشَةٍ مُّبَيِّنَةٍ يُضَعَفْ لَهَا الْعَذَابُ ضِعْفَيْنِ وكان ذالك على الله يسير المكان [الأحزاب: ٣٠] وثانيها : أن المحصن يرجم، وغيره يجلد. والثالث : أن العبد يحد نصف حد الحر. فثبت بما ذكرنا أنه لو صدر الذنب منهم لكان حالهم في استحقاق الذم العاجل والعقاب الآجل فوق حال عصاة الأمة، إلا أن هذا باطل بالإجماع؛ فإن أحدًا لا يجوز أن يقول : الرسول أخس حالا عند الله تعالى وأقل مرتبة من كل واحد من اللصوص والزناة، وهذا يدل على عدم صدور الذنب عنهم.
And what we say: The prophets—may God's blessings and peace be upon them—are infallible during the time of prophethood from major and minor sins intentionally; as for by forgetfulness, it is permissible. That which indicates the obligation of infallibility are several proofs. The first proof: If sin were to occur from them, their state in deserving blame immediately and punishment later would be more severe than the state of the sinners of the community, and this is false. Therefore, the occurrence of sin from them is also false. The explanation of the entailment: The greatest of God's blessings upon servants is His bestowing upon them the grace of messengership and prophethood. And whoever has more blessings from God Almighty upon him, the occurrence of sin from him is more heinous. Pure reason indicates this. Then it is confirmed from transmitted evidence by several aspects. One of them: His saying, 'O wives of the Prophet, you are not like any other women, if you are righteous' [33:32]; and 'Whoever of you commits a manifest indecency, the punishment will be doubled for her, and that is easy for God' [33:30]. The second: that the married person is stoned, while others are flogged. The third: that the slave is given half the punishment of a free person. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if sin were to occur from them, their state in deserving immediate blame and future punishment would be above the state of the sinners of the community, except that this is false by consensus; for no one may say: the Messenger is in a worse state before God Almighty and lower in rank than every single thief and fornicator. This indicates the non-occurrence of sin from them.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own position and his first proof. Razi states that prophets are infallible during the period of prophethood from major and minor sins when committed intentionally, but forgetfulness is permissible. He then provides the first proof, which is a rational argument: if a prophet committed a sin, he would deserve more blame and punishment than ordinary sinners because he has received the greatest blessings (prophethood). This is heinous in reason, and it is confirmed by scriptural evidence: the wives of the Prophet are addressed with a double punishment for sin (Surah al-Ahzab), the married person (muhsan) gets a harsher punishment (stoning) than the unmarried (flogging), and the slave gets half the punishment of a free person. All these show that greater grace implies greater punishment for sin. Therefore, if prophets sinned, they would be more blameworthy than common sinners. But it is consensually false to say the Prophet is worse than thieves and fornicators. Hence, prophets cannot sin.

Key Terms
عصمة
الكبيرة
الصغيرة
الذنب
النبوة
الرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are completely infallible in all actions, including unintentional ones.
Correction: Razi teaches that infallibility applies to intentional sins; forgetfulness is permissible, so unintentional lapses do not violate infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, prophets are infallible from which types of sins?
A) Only major sins
B) Only minor sins
C) Both major and minor sins intentionally
D) All sins including forgetfulness

Key Takeaway
Razi defines prophetic infallibility as protection from intentional major and minor sins, while forgetfulness is not a violation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you often excuse yourself by saying 'I forgot' or 'I didn't mean it'? In light of Razi's teaching that forgetfulness is permissible for prophets but intentional sin is not, how honestly do you assess the intention behind your own actions? Will you commit today to examining the real motive of your heart before making excuses?
?
If the prophets themselves were only guaranteed protection from intentional sins, how much more careful must you be about your own deliberate choices? What intentional sin are you currently tolerating that you know is wrong? What step will you take this week to abandon it, seeking God's help?
?
Razi's distinction shows that God's protection is about the heart's intent, not external perfection. Have you been relying on external rituals while neglecting the inner state of your soul? Where in your life is your intention mixed with hypocrisy or self-interest? What concrete change will you make today to purify your intention for Allah alone?

The First Proof: Greater Blessings Intensify Sin
Razi's first proof using rational and scriptural arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural argument that prophets cannot sin because their greater blessings make sin more heinous.

Core Idea
More divine grace means a sin is more severe, so prophets must be sinless.

Razi now presents his first proof for the necessity of prophetic infallibility. He argues from a rational principle: the more God has blessed someone, the more egregious their sin would be. Since prophets receive the greatest blessing—prophethood—any sin from them would be unimaginably heinous. He then supports this with three scriptural analogies. Let's examine this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the rational premise
Razi states that the one who receives greater blessings commits a more heinous sin. Understand why this is rationally necessary.
This is a principle of common sense: the more you owe, the worse the betrayal.
2
Examine the scriptural analogies
Razi gives three examples: the wives of the Prophet receive double punishment, the married person is stoned, and a slave gets half the punishment. Each shows that greater privilege means greater accountability.
The first analogy is directly from the Quran.
3
Apply to prophets
If prophets sinned, they would deserve more blame than the worst sinners. But that is unacceptable by consensus. Therefore, they cannot sin.
The conclusion is reached by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Proof: Prophetic Infallibility from Testimony
Razi provides a second proof: if prophets sinned, their testimony would be rejected according to Quranic ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for prophetic infallibility, which uses the Quranic ruling on accepting testimony from a fasiq to argue that prophets must be sinless.

Core Idea
A prophet's testimony must be accepted; a sinner's testimony is suspect.

Imam Razi continues his defense of prophetic infallibility with a second proof drawn from the Quran. He argues that if prophets could sin, they would be classified as fasiq, and the Quran commands us to verify news from such a person. This would undermine the very purpose of prophethood. Read the passage carefully, noting how Razi grounds his argument in a specific verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the passage is incomplete; it cuts off before the conclusion. Focus on the premise that is presented.
The source text ends with 'إلا أن' (except that...), indicating the conclusion is missing.
2
Identify the proof structure
Razi states a conditional: if sin occurred from prophets, their testimony would not be accepted. He then cites Quran 49:6 as evidence. Ask yourself: how does the verse support the conditional?
3
Connect the concept of fasiq to prophets
The verse commands caution when a fasiq brings news. If prophets could sin, they would be fasiq, and their prophetic testimony would require verification—contradicting their role as trustworthy messengers.
This is the missing logical conclusion implied by the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو صدر عنهم الذنب لما كانوا مقبولي الشهادة ؛ لقوله تعالى : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِن جَاءَكُمْ فَاسِقٌ بِنَبَاءٍ فَتَبَيَّنُوا أَن تُصِيبُوا قَوْمًا بِجَهَالَةٍ فَتُصْبِحُوا عَلَى مَا فَعَلْتُمْ نَادِمِينَ [الحجرات: ٦] أمرنا بالتثبت والتوقف في قبول شهادة الفاسق، إلا أن
The second proof: If sin were to occur from them, they would not be acceptable in testimony; because of His saying, 'O you who have believed, if a fasiq (sinner) comes to you with news, then verify it, lest you harm people out of ignorance and become regretful over what you have done' [49:6]. He commanded us to be cautious and to pause in accepting the testimony of the fasiq, except that...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage introduces the second proof for prophetic infallibility. Razi argues that if a prophet committed a sin, he would be considered a fasiq (sinner). The Quran (49:6) commands believers to verify news brought by a fasiq and not to accept his testimony without caution. Since prophets are bearers of divine testimony (revelation), they must be free from sin to ensure their testimony is accepted. The text cuts off before completing the argument, but the implication is that prophets cannot sin because otherwise their testimony (prophethood) would be rejected.

Scope of Prophetic Infallibility: Forgetfulness and Sin
Imam Razi discusses permissibility of forgetfulness and Rawafid absolute infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophets can experience forgetfulness and are blamed for it, and that the Rawafid (Shia) believe they are infallible from all sins including forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Prophets are not infallible from forgetfulness, but they are blameworthy for it.

Imam Razi begins by addressing the scope of prophetic infallibility. He explains that forgetfulness is permissible for prophets, yet they are still held accountable due to their higher knowledge. He then presents an extreme view that prohibits any sin, even unintentional, which is attributed to the Rawafid. This lesson unpacks these positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation to grasp the two main points: the permissibility of forgetfulness and the Rawafid doctrine.
Pay attention to the blame aspect – it contrasts with absolute infallibility.
2
Understand the blame for forgetfulness
Notice that even though forgetfulness is allowed, prophets are blamed because their knowledge is more complete. This shows a nuanced view of infallibility.
The blame is not punishment but a mark of higher responsibility.
3
Identify the Rawafid position
The Rawafid hold that no sin, small or great, is permissible for prophets under any circumstance. This is the most absolute view mentioned.
Contrast this with the earlier statement that forgetfulness is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السهو والنسيان فجائزان عليهم، ثم إنهم يعاتبون على ذلك السهو والنسيان، لما أن علومهم أكمل، فكان الواجب عليهم المبالغة في الصدق والتحفظ والتيقظ. الخامس : أن لا يجوز عليهم الصغيرة ولا الكبيرة لا بالعمد ولا بالتأويل ولا بالسهو ولا بالنسيان وهذا مذهب الروافض. واختلفت الأمة أيضًا في وقت وجوب هذه العصمة فالروافض قالت: إنها من أول الولادة إلى آخر العمر، وقال الأكثرون : هذه العصمة إنما تجب في زمان النبوة، وأما قبل النبوة فهي غير واجبة، وهو قول أكثر أصحابنا، وقول أبي الهذيل وأبي علي الجبائي. والذي نقوله : إن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - معصومون في زمان النبوة من الكبائر والصغائر بالعمد أما على سبيل السهو فجائز، ويدل على وجوب العصمة وجوه :
Forgetfulness and oversight are permissible for them, then they are blamed for that forgetfulness and oversight, because their knowledge is more complete, so it was incumbent upon them to be extreme in truthfulness, caution, and vigilance. The fifth: that [sins] small and great are not permissible for them, neither intentionally nor through interpretation nor through forgetfulness nor through oversight, and this is the doctrine of the Rawafid. The community also differed concerning the time when this infallibility is obligatory. The Rawafid said: it is from the beginning of birth until the end of life. The majority said: this infallibility is only obligatory during the time of prophethood, but before prophethood it is not obligatory, and this is the saying of most of our companions, and the saying of Abu Hudhayl and Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i. And what we say: the prophets – peace and blessings of God be upon them – are infallible during the time of prophethood from major and minor sins intentionally, but as for forgetfulness, it is permissible. And that which indicates the obligation of infallibility are proofs:
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the author's position on prophetic infallibility after reviewing differing opinions. It begins by noting that forgetfulness and oversight are permissible for prophets, yet they are blamed for them because of their complete knowledge. Then he lists the fifth opinion: the Rawafid (Shia) hold that no sin, small or great, is permissible for prophets in any circumstance. Next, he mentions disagreement on the timing of infallibility: the Rawafid say from birth to death, while the majority (including most of the author's school, Abu Hudhayl, and Abu Ali al-Jubba'i) say it is only during the period of prophethood. Finally, the author states his own view: prophets are infallible from major and minor intentional sins during prophethood, but forgetfulness is possible. He then announces that proofs will follow.
