First Objection on Adam's Sin and Its Answer
Imam al-Razi presents seven Qur'anic proofs of Adam's sin and three counter-arguments placing the sin before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key Qur'anic proofs used to argue that Adam sinned and how Imam al-Razi responds by situating the event before prophethood.

Core Idea
Adam's sin is reconciled by placing it before his prophethood.

This passage presents the first of two objections against prophetic infallibility, focusing on Adam. The objector marshals seven Qur'anic verses to show Adam committed a major sin. Imam al-Razi responds with three counter-arguments, all pointing to the same solution: the event occurred before Adam became a prophet. By understanding this exchange, you will see how kalam defends prophetic infallibility without denying Qur'anic texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Notice the structure: first the seven proofs, then the three counter-arguments.
The passage uses Qur'anic verses extensively.
2
Identify the seven proofs
List each of the seven proofs the objector uses. Note how each proof cites a specific verse and draws a conclusion about Adam's sin.
Proof 1: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord' implies blame.
3
Analyze the three counter-arguments
Study how Imam al-Razi responds. Notice that all three arguments converge on the same point: the sin happened before prophethood.
The third argument uses a logical analysis of messengership.
4
Reflect on the reconciliatory logic
Consider why this solution preserves prophetic infallibility. How does timing the sin before prophethood resolve the apparent contradiction?
This is a typical kalam method of tawfiq (reconciliation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : قصة زلة آدم.
وتمسكوا بهذه القصة من سبعة أوجه :
الأول : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَصَى آدَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى﴾ [طه: ۱۲۱] واسم العاصي ذم، فدل على أنه كان صاحب الكبيرة.
الثاني : قوله تعالى : ﴿فَغَوَى﴾ والغواية من الكبائر.
الثالث: أنه تاب، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَلَقَّى آدَمُ مِن رَّبِّهِ كَلِمَاتٍ فَتَابَ عَلَيْهِ﴾ [البقرة : ٣٧] وقوله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ اجْتَبَاهُ رَبُّهُ فَتَابَ عَلَيْهِ وَهَدَى﴾ [طه: ١٢٢] والتوبة عبارة عن الندم على المعصية، فإن كان صادقًا في ذلك فقد صدر الذنب عنه، وإن كان كاذبًا فيه كان أيضاً مذنباً؛ لأن الكذب ذنب.
الرابع: أنه ارتكب المنهي عنه بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ أَنْهَكُمَا عَن تِلْكُمَا الشَّجَرَةِ﴾ [الأعراف: ۲۲] ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبَا هَذِهِ الشَّجَرَةَ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۹] وارتكاب المنهي عنه ذنب.
الخامس : الله تعالى سماهما ظالمين، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَكُونَا مِنَ الظَّالِمِينَ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۹] وهما سميا أنفسهما بذلك؛ حيث قالا : ﴿رَبَّنَا ظَلَمْنَا أَنفُسَنَا﴾ [الأعراف: ۲۳] .
السادس : أن آدم اعترف بأنه لولا مغفرة الله لكان من الخاسرين، حيث قال : ﴿وَإِن لَّمْ تَغْفِرْ لَنَا وَتَرْحَمْنَا لَنَكُونَنَّ مِنَ الْخَاسِرِينَ﴾ [الأعراف : ٢٣] وهذا لا يليق إلا بصاحب الكبيرة.
السابع : أنه إنما خرج من الجنة بسبب وسوسة الشيطان وإزلاله بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَوَسْوَسَ لَهُمَا الشَّيْطَانُ﴾ [الأعراف: ۲۰] وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَزَلَّهُمَا الشَّيْطَانُ عَنْهَا فَأَخْرَجَهُمَا مِمَّا كَانَا فِيهِ﴾ [البقرة : ٣٦] وهذا يدل على أنه صاحب الكبيرة.
والجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذه الواقعة إنما وقعت قبل النبوة؟ ثم الذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك وجوه :
الأول: قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَعَصَى آدَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى ﴾ [ طه : ۱۲۱ - ۱۲۲] ثم قال: ﴿ثُمَّ اجْتَبَاهُ رَبُّهُ فَتَابَ عَلَيْهِ وَهَدَى﴾ فدل هذا على أن الاجتباء إنما حصل بعد واقعة الذنب ؛ لأن كلمة ثم للتراخي .
الثاني : لما دلت هذه الدلائل على صدور الذنب ودلت الدلائل التي ذكرناها على أن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لا يصدر عنهم الذنب حال كونهم أنبياء، لم يبق ههنا وجه في التوفيق، إلا أن تحمل هذه الواقعة على ما قبل النبوة.
الثالث : أنه لو كان رسولاً قبل الواقعة لكان إما أن يقال: إنه رسول إلى الملائكة، وهو باطل؛ لأن الملائكة رسل الله، لقوله : ﴿جَاعِلِ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا﴾ [فاطر: ١] والرسول لا يحتاج إلى رسول آخر - أو إلى البشر - وذلك أيضًا باطل؛ لأنه ما كان معه في الجنة من البشر إلا حواء، وأن الخطاب كان يأتيها من غير واسطة آدم، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبَا هَذِهِ الشَّجَرَةَ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۹] فإن هذا الخطاب خطاب معهما ابتداء، أو كان رسولا من غير مرسل إليه - وهو أيضًا باطل - فثبت أنها قبل هذه الواقعة ما كان موصوفا بالرسالة والنبوة.
The first objection: The story of Adam's slip. They hold on to this story from seven aspects: First: His statement, the Exalted: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and went astray' [Ta-Ha 121]; and the name 'disobedient' is blameworthy, so it indicates that he was a perpetrator of a major sin. Second: His statement, the Exalted: 'so he went astray' and going astray is among major sins. Third: That he repented, according to His statement, the Exalted: 'Then Adam received from his Lord words, and He turned toward him' [Al-Baqarah 37] and His statement, the Exalted: 'Then his Lord chose him, and He turned toward him and guided' [Ta-Ha 122]; and repentance is an expression of regret over sin. If he was truthful in that, then the sin has occurred from him; and if he was lying in it, he would also be a sinner because lying is a sin. Fourth: That he committed what was forbidden according to His statement, the Exalted: 'Did I not forbid you both from that tree?' [Al-A'raf 22] and 'Do not approach this tree' [Al-A'raf 19]; and committing what is forbidden is a sin. Fifth: Allah the Exalted called them 'wrongdoers' according to His statement: 'so you would be among the wrongdoers' [Al-A'raf 19], and they called themselves that when they said: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [Al-A'raf 23]. Sixth: That Adam acknowledged that without Allah's forgiveness he would be among the losers, when he said: 'And if You do not forgive us and have mercy upon us, we will surely be among the losers' [Al-A'raf 23]; and this is not befitting except for a perpetrator of a major sin. Seventh: That he left Paradise only because of the whispering of Satan and his causing them to slip, according to His statement: 'Then Satan whispered to them' [Al-A'raf 20] and His statement: 'So Satan caused them to slip from it and brought them out from what they were in' [Al-Baqarah 36]; and this indicates that he was a perpetrator of a major sin. The answer: Why may it not be said that this event occurred before prophethood? Then what indicates that it is so are aspects: First: His statement, the Exalted: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and went astray' [Ta-Ha 121-122], then He said: 'Then his Lord chose him, and He turned toward him and guided'; so this indicates that the selection occurred after the incident of sin, because the word 'thumma' indicates delay. Second: When these proofs have indicated the occurrence of sin, and the proofs we have mentioned indicate that the prophets - peace and blessings of Allah be upon them - do not commit sin while they are prophets, there remains no face of reconciliation here except to interpret this event as before prophethood. Third: That if he had been a messenger before the event, it would either be said: He is a messenger to the angels - and that is false because the angels are messengers of Allah, according to His statement: '[He] who makes the angels messengers' [Fatir 1] and a messenger does not need another messenger - or to humans - and that is also false because there was no human with him in Paradise except Hawwa', and the address came to her without the intermediary of Adam, as evidenced by His statement: 'Do not approach this tree' [Al-A'raf 19]; for this address was an address to both of them initially - or he was a messenger with no one sent to - and that is also false. So it is established that before this event he was not described with messengership and prophethood.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against the infallibility of prophets, focusing on the story of Adam. The objector lists seven Qur'anic proofs that Adam committed a major sin: (1) the verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and went astray' uses the blameworthy term 'disobeyed'; (2) 'going astray' is a major sin; (3) he repented, which implies sin; (4) he committed the forbidden act; (5) he was called a 'wrongdoer'; (6) he acknowledged needing forgiveness; (7) he was expelled due to Satan's deception. Each proof aims to show Adam committed a major sin, thus challenging his infallibility. Imam al-Razi responds with three arguments that the event occurred before his prophethood. First, the word 'thumma' (then) in the verse indicates that his selection (ijtiba') came after the sin, implying the sin was prior to prophethood. Second, since the proofs of sin exist and the proofs of prophetic infallibility are established, the only reconciliation is to place the sin before prophethood. Third, if he had been a messenger before the event, he would have had to be a messenger to angels (impossible) or to humans (but only Hawwa' was there, and Allah addressed both directly), or a messenger with no recipient (also impossible). Thus, the event occurred before he was a prophet or messenger.

Key Terms
عصيان
شبهة
كبيرة
توبة
نبوة
رسالة
آدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Qur'anic texts prove Adam sinned as a prophet, implying prophets can commit major sins.
Correction: The answer shows the sin occurred before Adam's prophethood, thus preserving the doctrine that prophets are infallible during their prophetic mission.

Checkpoint
In the third counter-argument, why does Imam al-Razi argue that Adam could not have been a messenger before the event?
A) Because messengers cannot sin
B) Because there is no one for him to be a messenger to
C) Because he was not yet created
D) Because the Quran explicitly says so

Key Takeaway
The apparent sin of Adam is reconciled by interpreting the event as occurring before his prophethood, thus preserving prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you assume that your past mistakes disqualify you from being chosen by Allah? How does Adam's story challenge or confirm that assumption?
?
Have you ever used a past mistake to justify ongoing disobedience, thinking 'I'm already a sinner'? How does the distinction between pre- and post-prophethood force you to reconsider the weight of your actions after coming to knowledge?
?
If Allah's selection of Adam came after his sin, what does that teach you about your own potential for redemption after failure? Are you living as if Allah's mercy is greater than your worst sin?

Refuting the Claim of Adam and Eve's Shirk
Imam al-Razi refutes the objection of shirk against Adam and Eve.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi refutes the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk by reinterpreting the Qur'anic verse as referring to pre-Islamic Arab customs.

Core Idea
The verse about 'one soul' refers to Qusayy, not Adam, thus protecting prophetic infallibility.

This lesson addresses a key objection to prophetic infallibility: the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk. Imam al-Razi provides a powerful refutation by showing that the Qur'anic verse actually refers to the Quraysh tribe and their idolatrous naming practices. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector cites Qur'an 7:189-190, interpreting 'one soul' as Adam and his spouse as Eve, and concludes that they committed shirk.
This objection challenges the infallibility of prophets.
2
Examine al-Razi's answer
Imam al-Razi denies that 'one soul' refers to Adam. Instead, he interprets the verse as addressing Quraysh, with 'one soul' being Qusayy, a noble ancestor, and the spouse being an Arab Qurashiyya.
This shifts the context from Adam to pre-Islamic Arab customs.
3
Grasp the implications
By reinterpreting the verse, al-Razi preserves the infallibility of Adam and Eve. The shirk mentioned refers to the descendants of Qusayy naming their children after idols.
This shows the importance of proper tafsir and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُم مِّن نَّفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۸۹] إلى قوله: ﴿فَتَعَالَى اللهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٩٠] قالوا: فالنفس الواحدة هذه، آدم وزوجها المخلوق منها هي حواء، وهذه الكنايات بأسرها عائدة إليهما فوجب أيضًا أن يكون قوله: ﴿فَجَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا فَتَعَالَى اللهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۹۰] عائدًا إليهما، وهذا يقتضي صدور الشرك عنهما.
والجواب: لا نسلم أن النفس المذكورة في الآية هي آدم، وليس في الآية ما يدل عليه، بل نقول: هذا الخطاب لقريش والأشراف منهم آل قصي، والمعنى: خلقكم من نفس قصي، وجعل من جنسها زوجها عربية قرشية ليسكن إليها، فلما آتاهما الله تعالى ما طلبا من الولد الصالح السوي سميا أولادهما بـ" عبد مناف" و " عبد العزى" و " عبد الدار " و " عبد قصي " فالضمير في قوله: "يشركون" لأعقابهما.
The second objection: They held on to His statement, the Exalted: 'It is He who created you from one soul' [Al-A'raf 189] up to His statement: 'Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him]' [Al-A'raf 190]. They said: So this one soul is Adam, and his spouse created from it is Hawwa', and all these pronouns refer to them both, so it must also be that His statement: 'But when He gave them a righteous child, they ascribed to Him partners in what He had given them. Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him]' [Al-A'raf 190] refers to them both, and this requires the commission of shirk from them. The answer: We do not accept that the soul mentioned in the verse is Adam, and there is nothing in the verse indicating that. Rather we say: This address is to Quraysh and their nobles, the clan of Qusayy, and the meaning: He created you from the soul of Qusayy, and made from its kind its spouse, an Arab Qurashiyya, that he might dwell with her. Then when Allah gave them what they sought of a righteous, sound child, they named their children 'Abd Manaf, 'Abd al-'Uzza, 'Abd al-Dar, and 'Abd Qusayy. So the pronoun in His saying 'they associate' refers to their descendants.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against prophetic infallibility, this time involving alleged shirk (associating partners with Allah). The objector cites verses from Surah al-A'raf (189-190) which speak of 'one soul' and its spouse, and then state that 'when He gave them a righteous child, they ascribed to Him partners'. The objector claims the 'one soul' is Adam and the spouse is Eve, so the shirk must be attributed to them. Imam al-Razi's answer is a decisive refutation: he rejects that the 'one soul' refers to Adam, noting that the verse provides no evidence for that. Instead, he interprets the verse as addressing the Quraysh tribe, specifically the clan of Qusayy. The 'one soul' is Qusayy (a noble ancestor), and the spouse is an Arab woman from Quraysh. When they had children, they named them after idols like 'Abd Manaf, 'Abd al-'Uzza, etc., which implies shirk in naming. Thus, the pronoun 'they associate' refers to the descendants of Qusayy, not to Adam and Eve. This interpretation avoids any implication of shirk against the prophets.

Key Terms
شرك
نفس واحدة
آدم
حواء
قريش
قصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that Qur'an 7:189-190 shows that Adam and Eve committed shirk (associating partners with Allah).
Correction: Imam al-Razi explains that the verse actually refers to the Quraysh tribe and their ancestor Qusayy, not Adam and Eve. The shirk mentioned is the practice of naming children after idols, which was done by the descendants of Qusayy.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, to whom does the pronoun 'they' in 'they associate' refer in verse 190?
A) Adam and Eve
B) The angels
C) The descendants of Qusayy
D) All of humanity

Key Takeaway
The verse does not refer to Adam and Eve; it refers to Quraysh, so there is no basis for claiming prophets committed shirk.

?

This lesson demands that you take responsibility for how you interpret the Book of Allah. What change will you make in your approach to Qur'anic study starting today?

Two Proofs that Adam's Sin Occurred Before Prophethood
Razi presents two arguments that Adam's sin occurred before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses logical reasoning to reconcile Adam's reported sin with the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness by demonstrating the sin occurred before his prophethood.

Core Idea
Adam's sin was before prophethood, preserving prophetic sinlessness.

Razi presents two rational arguments to resolve the apparent conflict between the reported sin of Adam and the established doctrine that prophets are sinless while prophets. These proofs rely on logical reconciliation and elimination of impossible scenarios, firmly placing the incident before Adam's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the numbered arguments (second and third).
2
Understand the first proof (second proof)
Identify how Razi reconciles evidence of sin with prophetic sinlessness by proposing the incident occurred before prophethood.
This is the only way to harmonize the two sets of evidence.
3
Understand the second proof (third proof)
Follow the elimination: if Adam were a messenger before the incident, he must have been sent to angels, humans, or no one. Each is impossible, so he was not a messenger then.
Pay attention to the scriptural evidence used.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how both proofs together establish that Adam was not a prophet or messenger at the time of the sin.
This preserves the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لما دلت هذه الدلائل على صدور الذنب ودلت الدلائل التي ذكرناها على أن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لا يصدر عنهم الذنب حال كونهم أنبياء، لم يبق ههنا وجه في التوفيق، إلا أن تحمل هذه الواقعة على ما قبل النبوة. الثالث: أنه لو كان رسولاً قبل الواقعة لكان إما أن يقال: إنه رسول إلى الملائكة، وهو باطل؛ لأن الملائكة رسل الله، لقوله: {جَاعِلُ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا} [فاطر: ١] والرسول لا يحتاج إلى رسول آخر - أو إلى البشر - وذلك أيضًا باطل؛ لأنه ما كان معه في الجنة من البشر إلا حواء، وأن الخطاب كان يأتيها من غير واسطة آدم، بدليل قوله تعالى: {وَلَا تَقْرَبَا هَذِهِ الشَّجَرَةَ} [الأعراف: ۱۹] فإن هذا الخطاب خطاب معهما ابتداء، أو كان رسولا من غير مرسل إليه - وهو أيضًا باطل - فثبت أنها قبل هذه الواقعة ما كان موصوفا بالرسالة والنبوة.
The second: When these proofs indicate the sin, and the proofs we mentioned indicate that prophets - peace and blessings be upon them - do not commit sin while being prophets, there remains no way of reconciliation except to interpret this incident as having occurred before prophethood. The third: If he was a messenger before the incident, then either it is said that he was a messenger to the angels, which is false because the angels are messengers of Allah, as He said: {Maker of the angels messengers} [Fatir:1], and a messenger does not need another messenger - or to humans - and that is also false because there was no human with him in Paradise except Eve, and the address used to come to her without the mediation of Adam, as evidenced by His statement: {And do not approach this tree} [Al-A'raf:19], for this address was a direct address to both of them from the beginning, or he was a messenger without anyone being sent to - and that is also false. Thus it is established that before this incident he was not described with messengership and prophethood.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the position that the sin of Adam (peace be upon him) occurred before his prophethood. The first argument (the second in sequence) states: since the evidence shows that a sin occurred, and the previously established proofs confirm that prophets do not sin while they are prophets, the only possible reconciliation is that this incident took place before Adam was a prophet. The second argument (the third) reasons by elimination: if Adam had been a messenger before the event, he must have been sent either to angels (impossible, as angels are themselves messengers and do not need a messenger), or to humans (impossible because Eve was the only human, and God addressed her directly without Adam's mediation), or he would be a messenger without any recipient (also impossible). Therefore, Adam was not a messenger or prophet before that event.

Key Terms
الذنب
النبوة
الرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets can commit sin after becoming prophets.
Correction: The passage argues that prophets are sinless while prophets, so any sin must have occurred before prophethood.

Checkpoint
What is the only way to reconcile evidence that a sin occurred with the principle that prophets do not sin while prophets?
A) The sin occurred after prophethood.
B) The sin occurred before prophethood.
C) The sin was not a real sin.
D) The sin was only a mistake.

Key Takeaway
Adam's sin must have occurred before his prophethood to preserve the principle of prophetic sinlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you secretly believe that your own flawed interpretations can override the clear proofs of their sinlessness? What would it take for you to submit completely to the prophetic message?

Refuting the Shirk Claim Against Adam and Eve
Razi refutes the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk based on Quran 7:189-190.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk by reinterpreting the Quranic verses to refer to Quraysh.

Core Idea
The Quranic verses about shirk refer to Quraysh, not to Adam and Eve.

This lesson tackles a significant objection against prophetic infallibility. Some claim that Quran 7:189-190 implies that Adam and Eve committed shirk. Imam Razi refutes this by showing that the verses actually address the Quraysh tribe and their ancestor Qusayy. Through careful analysis, he protects the prophets from any association with shirk.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
Read the doubt raised by the objectors. They interpret 'one soul' as Adam and Eve and the subsequent verse as referring to them making partners.
This is a common misconception.
2
Study Razi's answer
Read Razi's refutation. He denies that the soul is Adam and instead interprets it as Qusayy, the ancestor of Quraysh. He shows that the shirk mentioned is about naming children after idols, which applies to later generations.
Notice the shift in referent.
3
Analyze the implications
Consider how this interpretation preserves prophetic infallibility and demonstrates a method of tafsir that considers context and audience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: {هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُم مِّن نَّفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ} [الأعراف: 189] إلى قوله: {فَتَعَالَى اللهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ} [الأعراف: 190] قالوا: فالنفس الواحدة هذه آدم وزوجها المخلوق منها هي حواء، وهذه الكنايات بأسرها عائدة إليهما فوجب أيضًا أن يكون قوله: {جَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا} [الأعراف: 190] عائدًا إليهما، وهذا يقتضي صدور الشرك عنهما. والجواب: لا نسلم أن النفس المذكورة في الآية هي آدم، وليس في الآية ما يدل عليه، بل نقول: هذا الخطاب لقريش والأشراف منهم آل قصي، والمعنى: خلقكم من نفس قصي، وجعل من جنسها زوجها عربية قرشية ليسكن إليها، فلما آتاهما الله تعالى ما طلبا من الولد الصالح السوي سميا أولادهما بـ"عبد مناف" و"عبد العزى" و"عبد الدار" و"عبد قصي" فالضمير في قوله: "يشركون" لأعقابهما.
The second doubt: They held onto His statement: {It is He who created you from one soul} [Al-A'raf:189] up to His statement: {Exalted is Allah above what they associate} [Al-A'raf:190]. They said: This one soul is Adam, and his wife created from it is Eve, and all these pronouns refer to them, so it must be that His statement: {They made for Him partners in what He gave them} [Al-A'raf:190] refers to them, and this necessitates the occurrence of shirk from them. The answer: We do not concede that the soul mentioned in the verse is Adam; there is nothing in the verse to indicate that. Rather we say: This address is to Quraysh and the nobles among them, the clan of Qusayy. The meaning: He created you from the soul of Qusayy, and made from its kind its spouse, an Arab Qurayshi woman, that he might dwell with her. When Allah gave them what they asked for of righteous and sound offspring, they named their children 'Abd Manaf, 'Abd al-'Uzza, 'Abd al-Dar, and 'Abd Qusayy. So the pronoun in His statement "they associate" refers to their descendants.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt raised against prophetic infallibility using Surah Al-A'raf verses 189-190. The objectors claim that 'one soul' refers to Adam and Eve, and the subsequent verse about making partners refers to them, implying they committed shirk. The author refutes this by denying that the soul is Adam. He argues that the address is to Quraysh, specifically the lineage of Qusayy, and the meaning is that God created them from Qusayy's soul, and from its kind He made their spouses. When they were given righteous children, they named them with names containing 'Abd (servant) of various idols, thus the shirk is attributed to later descendants, not to Adam and Eve. This interpretation protects the prophets from any association with shirk.

Key Terms
شبهة
نفس
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quran 7:189-190 describes Adam and Eve committing shirk.
Correction: The verses actually refer to Quraysh and their ancestor Qusayy; the shirk mentioned is about naming children after idols, not an act of Adam and Eve.

Checkpoint
To whom does the author interpret the verse 'He created you from one soul' as referring?
A) Adam and Eve.
B) The Quraysh tribe and Qusayy.
C) All of humanity.
D) The angels.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk by reinterpreting the Quranic verses as referring to Quraysh and their ancestor Qusayy.

Refuting the First Doubt Against Prophet Noah
Razi presents and refutes the doubt that Noah lied about his son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the doubt that Prophet Noah lied about his son by analyzing the Quranic verses and the three major exegetical interpretations.

Core Idea
Noah's truthfulness is preserved through careful interpretation of the Quranic meaning of 'family'.

This lesson addresses the first of several doubts raised against Prophet Noah. The doubt claims that Noah contradicted Allah's statement, implying he lied. Razi presents three interpretive responses from the exegetes, showing that the apparent contradiction is resolved by understanding the context and nuances of the terms. Most importantly, the third interpretation is rejected to uphold the prophets' infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the doubt and the three responses. Notice the structure: doubt, then answer with three options.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses quoted and how they are used.
2
Identify the core doubt
The doubt is that Noah claimed his son was from his family, but Allah said he is not. This would imply a lie. Recognize how this challenges Noah's truthfulness.
3
Examine the first two interpretations
The first says the son is biological but 'not of your family' means not of the saved family or not of your religion. The second says the son was a stepson called 'son' due to association; evidence is Noah's phrasing 'min ahli' not 'minni'.
These interpretations preserve Noah's truthfulness by reinterpreting the negation.
4
Understand why the third is rejected
The third interpretation claims the son was illegitimate, but Razi rejects it because prophets must be protected from such disgrace. This shows the importance of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الثالثة: قصة نوح (الطليعة)[؟] وفيها شبهات: الشبهة الأولى: أنه قال عن نوح: {إِنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أَهْلِي} [هود: ٤٥] وقال تعالى: {إِنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنْ أَهْلِكَ إِنَّهُ عَمَلٌ غَيْرُ صَالِحٍ} [هود: ٤٦] وهذا يدل على أنه كذب في قوله: {إِنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أَهْلِي} [هود: ٤٥]. والجواب: المفسرون اختلفوا في هذا الابن على ثلاثة أقوال: الأول: أنه كان ابنا لصلبه بدليل قوله تعالى: {وَنَادَىٰ نُوحٌ ابْنَهُ} [هود: ٤٢] ثم اختلفوا في قوله: إنه ليس من أهلك، فقيل: ليس من أهلك الذين وعدتك أن أنجيهم معك، وقيل: ليس من أهل دينك، وهو قول ابن عباس وسعيد بن جبير. الثاني: أنه كان ابنا لامرأته إلا أنه لاختلاطه بأبنائه وأهل بيته أطلق اسم الابن عليه، كما أن إبليس لاختلاطه بالملائكة أطلق اسم الملك عليه. والدليل على ما ذكرناه قوله: {إن ابني من أهلي} ولم يقل: مني، وهذا مروي عن الباقر. الثالث: أنه ولد على فراشه من غير رشدة، وهو خطأ؛ لأنه يجب تنزيه الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - عن مثل هذه الفضيحة.
The third story: The story of Noah (al-Tali'ah?)[?] and it contains doubts: The first doubt: That he said about Noah: {Indeed my son is of my family} [Hud:45] and Allah said: {Indeed he is not of your family; indeed he is an unrighteous deed} [Hud:46]. This indicates that he lied in his saying {Indeed my son is of my family} [Hud:45]. The answer: The exegetes differed regarding this son on three sayings: First: He was his own son, evidenced by His statement: {And Noah called to his son} [Hud:42]. Then they differed concerning His saying: 'He is not of your family.' It was said: He is not of your family whom I promised to save with you. And it was said: He is not of your religion, which is the saying of Ibn Abbas and Sa'id ibn Jubayr. Second: He was the son of his wife, but due to his intermingling with his sons and household he was called 'son', just as Iblis, due to his intermingling with the angels, was called an angel. The evidence for what we mentioned is his saying: 'My son is of my family' and he did not say 'of me'. This is narrated from al-Baqir. Third: That he was born on his bed from an illegitimate union, and this is wrong because it is necessary to exalt the prophets - peace and blessings be upon them - from such a disgrace.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage addresses the first doubt against Noah. The doubt is that Noah claimed his son was of his family, but Allah said he is not of his family, which would imply a lie. The author presents three exegetical responses. The first: the son is indeed Noah's biological son, but the phrase 'not of your family' means either not of the family to be saved or not of your religion. The second: the son was a stepson, called 'son' due to close association, just as Iblis was called an angel because he mixed with them; evidence is that Noah said 'min ahli' (of my family) not 'minni' (from me). The third: the son was illegitimate, but this is rejected because prophets must be protected from such disgrace. Thus, the doubt is refuted, and Noah's truthfulness is preserved.

Key Terms
شبهة
ابن
أهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Noah actually lied about his son being from his family.
Correction: The apparent contradiction is resolved through exegesis: the son was either not of the saved family/religion, or was a stepson called 'son' by association. The third illegitimate option is rejected to protect prophetic infallibility.

Checkpoint
What evidence is given for the second interpretation that Noah's son was a stepson?
A) Allah said he is not of his family.
B) Noah said 'my son' but not 'from me'.
C) The son was a disbeliever.
D) The angels called Iblis a son.

Key Takeaway
Noah's truthfulness is defended by showing that the term 'family' in the verse can be understood in different ways, none of which require Noah to have lied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The third interpretation was rejected because it dishonors prophets. Is there any belief or action in your own life that, upon reflection, diminishes the honor of Allah's messengers? How will you correct it?

Second Doubt About Noah: His Question Being Wrong
Razi refutes the claim that Noah's question was sinful using Quranic analysis and grammatical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second doubt raised against Noah regarding his question, the Quranic evidence used, and Razi's refutation based on grammatical analysis and the principle of hypothetical prohibition.

Core Idea
Noah's question was not sinful; the Quranic prohibition was hypothetical, not actual.

The second doubt against Noah argues that his question to Allah about his son was a mistake, citing Quranic verses. Razi refutes this by examining the grammar of the verse and drawing an analogy with the Prophet Muhammad's own warning against shirk. This lesson unpacks the argument and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the structure: the doubt, the two Quranic proofs, the grammatical analysis, and the answer.
The passage is dense with grammatical detail; take your time.
2
Identify the two Quranic proofs
The doubt cites two verses from Surah Hud. Identify them and note what each one suggests about Noah's action.
The first is a direct prohibition, the second describes something as 'unrighteous'.
3
Analyze the grammatical argument
Understand the two recitations and the reasoning about the pronoun 'hu'. How does the author conclude that the pronoun refers to the question?
Pay attention to the principle that implicit mention (iḍmār) is contrary to the norm.
4
Examine Razi's answer
The answer denies that Noah claimed his son absolutely, and uses the analogy of the Prophet's warning against shirk. Grasp how this refutes the doubt.
The analogy shows that prohibition does not imply commission.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader theological principle: prophets are protected from sin, and Quranic warnings can be hypothetical. This lesson reinforces prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن سؤال نوح كان خطأ من وجهين : الأول : قوله تعالى: {فَلَا تَسْأَلْنِ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ إِنِّي أَعِظُكَ أَن تَكُونَ مِنَ الْجَهِلِينَ} [هود : ٤٦] . الثاني : قوله تعالى: {إِنَّهُ عَمَلٌ غَيْرُ صَالِحٍ} [هود : ٤٦] وفيه قراءتان: قراءة الكسائي على لفظ الماضي، والمعنى: أن ابنك عمل عملا غير صالح والباقون بالرفع والتنوين. والأول: مرجوح لأنه يقتضي إضمار الموصوف والإضمار خلاف الأصل، فثبتت القراءة الثانية - وهي الأولى - والهاء في قوله: "إنه" ضمير، فيكون عائدا إلى مذكور سابق، وهو إما السؤال وإما الابن، ولا يجوز عوده إلى الابن؛ لأن الابن لا يجوز وصفه بأنه عمل غير صالح، بل يكون عمل غير صالح، فيقتضي الإضمار، وهو خلاف الأصل، فوجب أن يكون الضمير عائدا إلى السؤال، فثبت أن ذلك السؤال غير صالح. والجواب: لا نسلم أنه ادعا لابنه مطلقا بل بشرط الإيمان. لا يقال: فلم قال الله تعالى: {فَلَا تَسْأَلْنِ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ} [هود : ٤٦] ؟ ولم قال: {وَإِنِّي أَعِظُكَ أَن تَكُونَ مِنَ الْجَهِلِينَ} [هود : ٤٦] ؟ ولم قال نوح: {إِنِّي أَعُوذُ بِكَ أَنْ أَسْأَلَكَ مَا لَيْسَ لِي بِهِ عِلْمٌ} [هود : ٤٧] لأنا نقول : لا يمتنع أن يكون نوح نهي عن ذلك، وإن لم يقع ذلك الفعل منه، كما أن رسولنا نهي عن الشرك في قوله تعالى: {لَئِنْ أَشْرَكْتَ لَيَحْبَطَنَّ عَمَلُكَ} [الزمر: ٦٥] وإن لم يقع ذلك منه.
The second doubt: That Noah's question was wrong from two aspects: First: His statement {So do not ask Me of that of which you have no knowledge. Indeed I advise you lest you be among the ignorant} [Hud:46]. Second: His statement {Indeed it is an unrighteous deed} [Hud:46]. There are two recitations: the recitation of al-Kisa'i in the past tense form, meaning your son did an unrighteous deed, and the rest with raf' and tanwin. The first is preponderated because it requires implicit mention of the described thing, and implicit mention is contrary to the principle. So the second recitation is established - and it is the first [mentioned?]. The pronoun in 'innahu' is a pronoun, so it refers to a previously mentioned thing, which is either the question or the son. It is not permissible for it to refer to the son because the son cannot be described as 'an unrighteous deed', but rather would be [the doer of] an unrighteous deed, which requires implicit mention, contrary to principle. So the pronoun must refer to the question, thus it is established that that question was unrighteous. The answer: We do not concede that he claimed [the son] absolutely, but rather with the condition of faith. It should not be said: So why did Allah say {Do not ask Me of that of which you have no knowledge}? And why did He say {And I advise you lest you be among the ignorant}? And why did Noah say {I seek refuge in You from asking You that of which I have no knowledge}? For we say: It is not impossible that Noah was prohibited from that, even though he did not actually do it, just as our Messenger was prohibited from shirk in His statement {If you associate [others with Allah], your work would surely become worthless} [Az-Zumar:65] even though he did not do it.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the second doubt against Noah, claiming his question was sinful, supported by two Quranic references. The first is Allah's prohibition and admonishment. The second involves a grammatical analysis of the recitation of Hud 46. The author notes two readings: al-Kisa'i reads it as a past tense verb (making the son the doer of an unrighteous deed), while others read it as a nominal sentence (the son is an unrighteous deed). The author argues that the first reading is weak because it requires an implied object, contrary to normal principles. Therefore, the pronoun 'hu' in 'innahu' refers back to an earlier noun, which could be the question or the son. It cannot refer to the son because a person cannot be described as 'an unrighteous deed' without ellipsis. Thus the pronoun refers to the question, proving the question itself was unrighteous. The answer denies that Noah claimed his son absolutely; rather, he claimed him conditional on faith. Moreover, the prohibition and admonition do not necessarily mean Noah committed the act; just as the Prophet Muhammad was warned against shirk but never did it, Noah was warned against a hypothetical wrong but did not commit it.

سؤال
قراءة
ضمير
إضمار

Misconception: That the Quranic prohibition implies Noah actually committed a sin.
Correction: The prohibition was a warning to avoid a hypothetical action, not evidence that Noah actually sinned. The analogy with the Prophet Muhammad's warning against shirk shows that such prohibitions can be preventative.

Checkpoint
According to the second doubt, what does the pronoun in 'innahu' in Hud 46 refer to?
A) The son.
B) The question.

D) The family.

Key Takeaway
Noah's question was not sinful; the Quranic prohibition was a precautionary warning, not an indication of actual sin.

?

?
The Prophet was warned against shirk even though he never committed it — a reminder that divine warnings can be both real protection and mercy. Right now, is there a sin you have avoided not because you feared Allah's command but because circumstances prevented it? How can you transform that into a genuine, heartfelt obedience?

Defending Noah's Infallibility: Refuting the Objection of His Question
Imam Razi refutes the objection that Noah's question was an error using Qur'anic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that Noah's question to Allah was an error, using arguments about conditional requests and precautionary warnings.

Core Idea
A prophetic reproach can be a warning, not a proof of sin.

This passage presents a critical objection against the infallibility of prophets: Noah's question to save his son seems to be rebuked in the Quran. Imam Razi responds with a nuanced defense, showing that the reproach is a preventive caution, not an indictment of an actual mistake. By analyzing the Quranic verses and variant readings, he demonstrates that the objection rests on weak evidence, while the proofs of prophetic infallibility are stronger.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the beginning of the passage to grasp the objector's claim: that Noah's question was a mistake, based on two Quranic verses.
The objector uses both the explicit reproach and the phrase 'innahu 'amalun ghayru salih'.
2
Examine the two variant readings
Notice the discussion of the reading of al-Kisai and the majority reading. Understand why the objector favors the second reading.
This grammatical analysis is crucial for the objector's argument that the pronoun refers to the question.
3
Follow Imam Razi's first answer
Razi denies that Noah made an unconditional request; he asked only if his son was a believer. He then draws an analogy with the Prophet Muhammad being warned against shirk.
This shows that a warning does not imply the warned act occurred.
4
Analyze the second answer
Razi rejects the objector's claim about the pronoun as weak evidence, asserting that stronger proofs of infallibility require a different interpretation.
This step emphasizes the hierarchy of evidence in theological argumentation.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how Razi harmonizes the apparent conflict between the verse and the doctrine of infallibility.
The key is to see that divine reproach can be precautionary, not punitive.

الشبهة الثانية : أن سؤال نوح كان خطأ الأول : قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَلَا تَاتِنِي مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ إِنِّي أَعِظُكَ أَن تَكُونَ مِنَ الْجَاهِلِينَ ﴾ [هود : ٤٦] . من وجهين : الثاني : قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّهُ عَمَلٌ غَيْرُ صَالِحٍ ﴾ [هود : ٤٦] وفيه قراءتان: قراءة الكسائي على لفظ الماضي، والمعنى: أن ابنك عمل عملا غير صالح والباقون بالرفع والتنوين. والأول: مرجوح لأنه يقتضي إضمار الموصوف والإضمار خلاف الأصل، فثبتت القراءة الثانية - وهي الأولى - والهاء في قوله : "إنه" ضمير، فيكون عائدا إلى مذكور سابق، وهو إما السؤال وإما الابن، ولا يجوز عوده إلى الابن ؛ لأن الابن لا يجوز وصفه بأنه عمل غير صالح، بل يكون عمل غير صالح، فيقتضي الإضمار، وهو خلاف الأصل، فوجب أن يكون الضمير عائدا إلى السؤال، فثبت أن ذلك السؤال غير صالح. والجواب: لا نسلم أنه ادعا لابنه مطلقا بل بشرط الإيمان. لا يقال: فلم قال الله تعالى : ﴿ فَلَا تَاتِنِي مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ ﴾ [هود : ٤٦] ؟ ولم قال : ﴿ إِنِّي أَعِظُكَ أَن تَكُونَ مِنَ الْجَاهِلِينَ ﴾ [هود : ٤٦] ؟ ولم قال نوح : ﴿ إِنِّي أَعُوذُ بِكَ أَنْ أَسْأَلَكَ مَا لَيْسَ لِي بِهِ عِلْمٌ ﴾ [هود : ٤٧]. لأنا نقول : لا يمتنع أن يكون نوح عليه السلام قد نهي عن ذلك، وإن لم يقع ذلك الفعل منه، كما أن نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم نهي عن الشرك في قوله تعالى : ﴿ لَئِنْ أَشْرَكْتَ لَيَحْبَطَنَّ عَمَلُكَ ﴾ [الزمر: ٦٥] وإن لم يقع ذلك منه. وقوله ثانيًا : إن الموصوف بأنه عمل غير صالح هو السؤال، قلنا: لا نسلم، بل هو دليل ضعيف، والأدلة الدالة على عصمة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - أقوى من ذلك، فوجب المصير إلى إثبات هذا الإضمار توفيقا بين هذه الآية وبين الآيات الدالة على ثبوت عصمة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم.
The second objection: that Noah's question was a mistake. First: His statement exalted is: "So do not ask Me for that of which you have no knowledge. Indeed, I advise you lest you be among the ignorant." [Hud 11:46] In two aspects: Second: His statement exalted is: "Indeed, it is a deed not righteous." [Hud 11:46] And there are two readings: the reading of al-Kisai on the past tense, and the meaning is: your son committed an unrighteous deed, and the rest with nominative and nunation. And the first is weak because it requires a hidden described noun, and hiding is contrary to the default, so the second reading—which is the first—is established. The pronoun in "innahu" is a pronoun referring to a prior mentioned thing, which is either the question or the son. It is not permissible for it to refer to the son because the son cannot be described as "it is a deed not righteous"; rather it would be "a deed not righteous," which requires a hidden noun, contrary to the default. So the pronoun must refer to the question, thus it is established that that question was not righteous. And the answer: We do not concede that he claimed a son absolutely, but rather conditionally on faith. It cannot be said: Then why did Allah say: "So do not ask Me..."? And why did He say: "Indeed, I advise you..."? And why did Noah say: "Indeed, I seek refuge in You from asking You that of which I have no knowledge"? For we say: It is not impossible that Noah, peace be upon him, was forbidden from that, even though that act did not occur from him, just as our Prophet, peace be upon him, was forbidden from shirk in His statement: "If you associate partners, your work would surely become worthless" [az-Zumar 39:65] even though it did not occur from him. And his statement secondly: That the thing described as "a deed not righteous" is the question—we say: we do not concede; rather it is a weak evidence, and the proofs indicating the infallibility of the prophets—peace and blessings be upon them—are stronger than that. So it is necessary to resort to establishing this hidden noun to harmonize between this verse and the verses indicating the establishment of the infallibility of the prophets—peace and blessings be upon them.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument defending the prophet Noah against the claim that his intercession for his son was an error. The passage begins by stating the second objection: that Noah's question to Allah to save his son was a mistake. The objector provides two Qur'anic evidences. First, the verse: "So do not ask Me for that of which you have no knowledge" (Hud 11:46). The objector sees this as a reproach indicating that Noah committed an error by asking. Second, the verse: "Indeed, it is a deed not righteous" (Hud 11:46). The objector discusses two readings: al-Kisai's reading treats 'amal as past tense, meaning 'your son committed an unrighteous deed,' while the other reading treats it as a nominal sentence with nunation. The objector argues that the first reading is weak because it requires a hidden described noun, while the second reading is stronger. Then the objector argues that the pronoun in 'innahu' (indeed it) must refer to the question, not the son, because describing the son as 'a deed not righteous' is not appropriate; rather it would require a hidden noun. Thus the objector concludes that the question itself was unrighteous. Imam Razi answers first by denying the absolute claim: Noah did not ask for his son unconditionally, but only under the condition of faith. Then he addresses the objection that the reproach implies a prior error. Razi argues that it is possible that Noah was merely warned against something he did not actually do, just as the Prophet Muhammad was warned against shirk although he never committed it. Thus the divine reproach is a caution, not a rebuke for an actual sin. Finally, Razi rejects the objector's second point about the pronoun, calling it weak evidence, and insists that the stronger proofs of prophetic infallibility require us to assume a hidden noun (i.e., that the phrase means 'your son is a person of unrighteous deed') to harmonize the verses.

Correction: This passage shows that a reproach can be a preventive warning, not evidence of an actual sin, as demonstrated by the analogy of the Prophet being warned against shirk.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defends Noah's infallibility by showing that the Quranic reproach is a preventive warning, not evidence of an actual error, and that variant readings and careful grammatical analysis support this interpretation.

The First Doubt: 'This is My Lord'
Razi answers the first doubt concerning Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that Abraham committed shirk by saying 'This is my Lord', showing it was a hypothetical argument, not a belief.

Core Idea
Abraham's 'This is my Lord' was a rhetorical hypothesis, not an act of shirk.

Imam Razi begins addressing the doubts against Abraham's prophethood. The first doubt involves his statement in the Quran, 'This is my Lord'. Some may think Abraham was actually worshipping a celestial body. Razi explains that this was said hypothetically to refute the polytheists' belief, using an analogy from theological debates. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the first doubt and its answer. Let the text speak before analyzing.
Notice the phrase 'على سبيل الفرض'.
2
Identify the hypothesis
Razi states that Abraham said 'This is my Lord' as a hypothesis (فرض). Understand that he was not asserting it as his own belief.
Hypothesis in kalam is a common method to refute opponents.
3
Analyze the analogy
Razi compares to a debater refuting the pre-eternity of bodies: first state the opponent's claim, then show its contradiction. See how that applies to the verse.
The analogy clarifies the method: quote then refute.
4
Connect to the verse
After saying 'This is my Lord', Abraham says 'I do not like those that set.' This is the refutation: if it were a lord, it would not change.
This shows Abraham's intention was not worship but argument.
5
Reflect on the significance
Reflect on why this refutation is important for defending Abraham's prophethood and tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الرابعة : قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام وفيها ثمان شبهات : الشبهة الأولى : قوله تعالى: هَذَا رَبِّي ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٦] والجواب : ذكر هذا على سبيل الفرض ليبطل به ذلك، كالواحد منا إذا أراد أن يبطل القول بقدم الأجسام، فيقول أولا : الجسم قديم أي هكذا يقول الخصم ثم يقول : لو كان قديما لم يكن متغيرا، فكذا ههنا : قال هَذَا رَبِّي أي كذا يقولون، ثم قال لا أحب الآفلين [الأنعام: ٧٦] أي لو كان ربا لم يتغير. الشبهة الثانية : قال : وَبَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ [الأنبياء : ٦٣] وهذا كذب. والجواب من وجوه : الأول: أن قصد إبراهيم عليه السلام ما كان أن ينسب ذلك الصادر عنه إلى الصنم، وإنما قصد تقريره لنفسه على أسلوب تعريضي به وهذا كما لو قال صاحبك ـ وقد كنت كتبت كتابا بخط حسن وأنت مشهور بحسن الخط أنت كتبت هذا، وصاحبك أمي لا يحسن الخط، ولا يقدر إلا على حروف خربشة فاسدة، فقلت له : بل كتبته أنت، وكان قصدك بهذا الجواب تقريره لك مع الاستهزاء به، لا نفيه عنك، وإثباته للأمي؛ لأن إثباته والآمر دائر بينهما للعجز استهزاء به وإثبات للقادر. ثانيها : أن إبراهيم غاظته تلك الأصنام، حين أبصرها مزينة، وكان غيظه من كبيرها أشد لما رأى من زيادة تعظيمهم له، فأسند الفعل إليه ؛ لأنه هو السبب في استهانته بها وحطمه لها، والفعل كما يسند إلى المباشر فقد يسند إلى السبب الحامل عليه. وثالثها: يجوز أن يكون فيه وقف عند قوله : بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ [الأنبياء : ٢١] ثم يبتدئ فيقول : فَاسْأَلُوهُمْ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٦٣] والمعنى: بل فعله كبيرهم، وعنى به نفسه؛ لأن الإنسان أكبر من كل صنم.
The fourth story: The story of Abraham, peace be upon him, and it contains eight doubts: The first doubt: His statement exalted is: 'This is my Lord' [al-An'am 6:76]. And the answer: He mentioned this by way of hypothesis to refute thereby, like one of us when he wants to refute the statement of the pre-eternity of bodies, he first says: 'The body is pre-eternal' — that is, thus the opponent says — then he says: 'If it were pre-eternal, it would not change.' So similarly here: He said 'This is my Lord' — that is, thus they say — then He said 'I do not like those that set' [al-An'am 6:76] — that is, if He were Lord, He would not change. The second doubt: He said: 'Rather, their chief did it' [al-Anbiya' 21:63] and this is a lie. And the answer is from aspects: First: The intention of Abraham, peace be upon him, was not to attribute that action to the idol, but rather he intended to establish it for himself in a style of indirect expression. This is like if your companion—while you had written a book in beautiful handwriting and were famous for fine handwriting—said: 'You wrote this,' and your companion is illiterate, cannot write well, and can only produce scrawled defective letters, so you said to him: 'Rather, you wrote it.' Your intention with this answer was to affirm it for yourself with ridicule of him, not to deny it from yourself and affirm it for the illiterate; because affirming it—when the matter is confined between the two due to inability—is ridicule of him and affirmation of the capable. Second: That Abraham became angry at those idols when he saw them adorned, and his anger at their chief was greater because he saw their excessive veneration of it, so he ascribed the action to it because it was the cause of his contempt for them and his smashing them. The action, just as it is ascribed to the direct agent, may also be ascribed to the motivating cause. Third: It is possible that there is a pause at his statement: 'Rather, their chief did it' [al-Anbiya' 21:21?] then he begins saying: 'So ask them' [al-Anbiya' 21:63] and the meaning: 'Rather, their chief did it,' meaning himself, because a human is greater than any idol.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a classification of doubts against Abraham's prophethood, presenting the first two doubts and their refutations. The passage begins by introducing the fourth story (of Abraham) and stating that it contains eight doubts. The first doubt is from the verse 'This is my Lord' (al-An'am 6:76), which seems to indicate that Abraham initially worshipped a celestial body. Imam Razi answers that this was said hypothetically ('ala sabili al-fard) in order to refute the polytheists' belief. He draws an analogy: if a theologian wants to refute the claim that bodies are pre-eternal, he might first state the opponent's claim ('The body is pre-eternal') and then argue that if it were pre-eternal, it would not change, thus proving it false. So Abraham said 'This is my Lord' to quote the polytheists' belief, and then said 'I do not like those that set' (al-An'am 6:76) to explain that if that object were a lord, it would not change and set. Therefore, Abraham was not actually believing in it as his Lord. The second doubt is from the verse 'Rather, their chief did it' (al-Anbiya' 21:63) when Abraham was asked if he smashed the idols. This could be seen as a lie. Imam Razi gives three answers. The first answer: Abraham's intention was not to actually attribute the act to the idol, but to use indirect expression (ta'arid) to assert his own action with ridicule. He gives an analogy: if a skilled calligrapher is falsely accused by an illiterate person of writing a beautiful book, the calligrapher might sarcastically say to the illiterate, 'Rather, you wrote it,' to emphasize his own skill and mock the accuser. Similarly, Abraham attributed the act to the chief idol sarcastically. The second answer: Abraham was angry at the idols when he saw them adorned. His anger was greatest at the chief idol because of the people's excessive veneration of it. So he ascribed the act to it because it was the cause of his anger and his smashing of all idols. Actions can be attributed to the cause as well as to the direct agent. The third answer: There may be a pause in the verse after 'Rather, their chief did it,' and then the sentence begins anew: 'So ask them' (fis'alūhum). The meaning would then be: 'Rather, their chief did it' — meaning himself, because a human is greater than any idol. This interpretation avoids attributing the action to the idol and instead indicates that Abraham himself was the doer.

Key Terms
الفرض (hypothesis)
الآفلين (those that set)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abraham committed shirk by saying 'This is my Lord'.
Correction: Abraham said this as a hypothesis to refute the polytheists, similar to a debater stating an opponent's view before disproving it.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what was the purpose of Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord'?
A) To declare his own belief in a celestial lord.
B) To quote the polytheists' claim as a hypothesis before refuting it.
C) To deceive the polytheists into thinking he agreed with them.
D) To express his confusion about who his Lord was.

Key Takeaway
Abraham's 'This is my Lord' was a rhetorical hypothesis to refute shirk, not an act of shirk itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue with someone about religious beliefs, do you ever use the same kind of hypothetical reasoning that Abraham used? If not, what holds you back from engaging intellectually with others about your faith?
?
Have you ever been misunderstood when speaking about your faith, perhaps because someone took your words literally when you were speaking hypothetically? How can you learn from Abraham's example to clarify your intent before being judged?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone is the unchanging Lord, what in your life still 'sets' or changes in a way that competes with your devotion to Him? Are you willing to let go of those shifting attachments today?

The Second Doubt: 'Rather, Their Chief Did It'
Razi provides three refutations of the charge that Abraham lied by saying 'Rather, their chief did it'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways Imam Razi refutes the charge that Abraham lied when he said 'Rather, their chief did it' about smashing the idols.

Core Idea
Abraham's statement was not a lie but a rhetorical device, causal attribution, or a pause for emphasis.

The second doubt against Abraham's prophethood stems from his statement in the Quran, 'Rather, their chief did it,' when asked about smashing the idols. Critics claim this was a lie. Imam Razi provides three distinct answers to defend Abraham's truthfulness. This lesson explores each answer, showing how they fit within Islamic theology and rhetorical practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the second doubt
Read the Arabic and translation of the second doubt and its three answers. Begin with the doubt itself.
2
Understand the first answer: indirect expression
Razi says Abraham used 'تعريض' (indirect expression/insinuation) to mock the polytheists. Consider the analogy of the calligrapher and the illiterate person.
This is a form of sarcasm, not a lie.
3
Understand the second answer: causal attribution
Abraham ascribes the act to the chief idol because it was the cause of his anger. Actions can be attributed to the motivating cause as well as the direct agent.
Concept of السبب والمباشر in legal theory.
4
Understand the third answer: pause and re-interpretation
There may be a pause after 'بل فعله كبيرهم' and the next sentence starts 'فاسألوهم'. Then 'كبيرهم' refers to Abraham himself, as a human is greater than idols.
This reading avoids any false attribution.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these answers protect Abraham's prophethood and demonstrate the depth of Quranic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصة الرابعة : قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام وفيها ثمان شبهات : الشبهة الأولى : قوله تعالى: هَذَا رَبِّي ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٦] والجواب : ذكر هذا على سبيل الفرض ليبطل به ذلك، كالواحد منا إذا أراد أن يبطل القول بقدم الأجسام، فيقول أولا : الجسم قديم أي هكذا يقول الخصم ثم يقول : لو كان قديما لم يكن متغيرا، فكذا ههنا : قال هَذَا رَبِّي أي كذا يقولون، ثم قال لا أحب الآفلين [الأنعام: ٧٦] أي لو كان ربا لم يتغير. الشبهة الثانية : قال : وَبَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ [الأنبياء : ٦٣] وهذا كذب. والجواب من وجوه : الأول: أن قصد إبراهيم عليه السلام ما كان أن ينسب ذلك الصادر عنه إلى الصنم، وإنما قصد تقريره لنفسه على أسلوب تعريضي به وهذا كما لو قال صاحبك ـ وقد كنت كتبت كتابا بخط حسن وأنت مشهور بحسن الخط أنت كتبت هذا، وصاحبك أمي لا يحسن الخط، ولا يقدر إلا على حروف خربشة فاسدة، فقلت له : بل كتبته أنت، وكان قصدك بهذا الجواب تقريره لك مع الاستهزاء به، لا نفيه عنك، وإثباته للأمي؛ لأن إثباته والآمر دائر بينهما للعجز استهزاء به وإثبات للقادر. ثانيها : أن إبراهيم غاظته تلك الأصنام، حين أبصرها مزينة، وكان غيظه من كبيرها أشد لما رأى من زيادة تعظيمهم له، فأسند الفعل إليه ؛ لأنه هو السبب في استهانته بها وحطمه لها، والفعل كما يسند إلى المباشر فقد يسند إلى السبب الحامل عليه. وثالثها: يجوز أن يكون فيه وقف عند قوله : بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ [الأنبياء : ٢١] ثم يبتدئ فيقول : فَاسْأَلُوهُمْ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٦٣] والمعنى: بل فعله كبيرهم، وعنى به نفسه؛ لأن الإنسان أكبر من كل صنم.
The fourth story: The story of Abraham, peace be upon him, and it contains eight doubts: The first doubt: His statement exalted is: 'This is my Lord' [al-An'am 6:76]. And the answer: He mentioned this by way of hypothesis to refute thereby, like one of us when he wants to refute the statement of the pre-eternity of bodies, he first says: 'The body is pre-eternal' — that is, thus the opponent says — then he says: 'If it were pre-eternal, it would not change.' So similarly here: He said 'This is my Lord' — that is, thus they say — then He said 'I do not like those that set' [al-An'am 6:76] — that is, if He were Lord, He would not change. The second doubt: He said: 'Rather, their chief did it' [al-Anbiya' 21:63] and this is a lie. And the answer is from aspects: First: The intention of Abraham, peace be upon him, was not to attribute that action to the idol, but rather he intended to establish it for himself in a style of indirect expression. This is like if your companion—while you had written a book in beautiful handwriting and were famous for fine handwriting—said: 'You wrote this,' and your companion is illiterate, cannot write well, and can only produce scrawled defective letters, so you said to him: 'Rather, you wrote it.' Your intention with this answer was to affirm it for yourself with ridicule of him, not to deny it from yourself and affirm it for the illiterate; because affirming it—when the matter is confined between the two due to inability—is ridicule of him and affirmation of the capable. Second: That Abraham became angry at those idols when he saw them adorned, and his anger at their chief was greater because he saw their excessive veneration of it, so he ascribed the action to it because it was the cause of his contempt for them and his smashing them. The action, just as it is ascribed to the direct agent, may also be ascribed to the motivating cause. Third: It is possible that there is a pause at his statement: 'Rather, their chief did it' [al-Anbiya' 21:21?] then he begins saying: 'So ask them' [al-Anbiya' 21:63] and the meaning: 'Rather, their chief did it,' meaning himself, because a human is greater than any idol.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a classification of doubts against Abraham's prophethood, presenting the first two doubts and their refutations. The passage begins by introducing the fourth story (of Abraham) and stating that it contains eight doubts. The first doubt is from the verse 'This is my Lord' (al-An'am 6:76), which seems to indicate that Abraham initially worshipped a celestial body. Imam Razi answers that this was said hypothetically ('ala sabili al-fard) in order to refute the polytheists' belief. He draws an analogy: if a theologian wants to refute the claim that bodies are pre-eternal, he might first state the opponent's claim ('The body is pre-eternal') and then argue that if it were pre-eternal, it would not change, thus proving it false. So Abraham said 'This is my Lord' to quote the polytheists' belief, and then said 'I do not like those that set' (al-An'am 6:76) to explain that if that object were a lord, it would not change and set. Therefore, Abraham was not actually believing in it as his Lord. The second doubt is from the verse 'Rather, their chief did it' (al-Anbiya' 21:63) when Abraham was asked if he smashed the idols. This could be seen as a lie. Imam Razi gives three answers. The first answer: Abraham's intention was not to actually attribute the act to the idol, but to use indirect expression (ta'arid) to assert his own action with ridicule. He gives an analogy: if a skilled calligrapher is falsely accused by an illiterate person of writing a beautiful book, the calligrapher might sarcastically say to the illiterate, 'Rather, you wrote it,' to emphasize his own skill and mock the accuser. Similarly, Abraham attributed the act to the chief idol sarcastically. The second answer: Abraham was angry at the idols when he saw them adorned. His anger was greatest at the chief idol because of the people's excessive veneration of it. So he ascribed the act to it because it was the cause of his anger and his smashing of all idols. Actions can be attributed to the cause as well as to the direct agent. The third answer: There may be a pause in the verse after 'Rather, their chief did it,' and then the sentence begins anew: 'So ask them' (fis'alūhum). The meaning would then be: 'Rather, their chief did it' — meaning himself, because a human is greater than any idol. This interpretation avoids attributing the action to the idol and instead indicates that Abraham himself was the doer.

Key Terms
التعريض (indirect expression)
الاستهزاء (ridicule)
السبب (cause)
المباشر (direct agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abraham told a lie when he said the chief idol broke the other idols.
Correction: Imam Razi provides three valid interpretations that show Abraham did not lie: sarcastic indirection, causal attribution, or referring to himself as 'their chief'.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Imam Razi's answers to the second doubt?
A) Abraham used indirect expression (تعريض) to mock the polytheists.
B) Abraham attributed the act to the chief idol as the cause of his anger.
C) Abraham admitted he was lying to save himself from punishment.
D) There is a pause in the verse, and 'their chief' refers to Abraham himself.

Key Takeaway
Abraham's statement 'Rather, their chief did it' is not a lie but a rhetorical, causal, or self-referential expression that accords with his truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a rhetorical statement in a tense situation to avoid a direct lie? Does that make the statement true or false in your conscience? How does Abraham's example challenge you to be truthful even when ridiculing falsehood?
?
Abraham's anger at the idols led him to attribute the act to the chief idol as a cause. When you are angry, do you ever attribute actions to others in ways that are not fully accurate? How can you guard against letting anger distort your speech?
?
The third refutation suggests Abraham called himself 'their chief' because a human is greater than any idol. Do you live with the awareness that you are created greater than anything you might worship besides Allah? What changes if you truly internalize that?

Was Ibrahim's 'This is my Lord' a Claim of Divinity?
Razi refutes the objection that Ibrahim's statement was a claim of divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends Ibrahim's statement as a rhetorical hypothesis rather than a declaration of belief.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's statement was a hypothesis to refute, not a belief.

This lesson addresses a common objection to Ibrahim's prophethood: his statement 'This is my Lord' (Quran 6:76). Critics argue this shows he attributed divinity to a star. Razi clarifies that Ibrahim was employing a rhetorical device—stating the opponent's view in order to refute it. This passage is essential for understanding how Islamic scholars defend the prophets from apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: objection first, then answer.
The answer uses the concept of 'farḍ' (hypothesis).
2
Understand the analogy
Razi compares Ibrahim's method to a theologian refuting the pre-eternity of bodies. Recognize the pattern: stating the opponent's case before disproving it.
This shows Ibrahim was not endorsing the belief, but exposing its flaw.
3
Connect to the proof
Ibrahim's proof is in the second part: 'I do not like those that set.' This demonstrates that a changing object cannot be a true Lord.
The setting of the star is evidence against its divinity.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why some might misunderstand Ibrahim's words. The lesson guards against the false belief that Ibrahim ever worshipped created things.
This defense maintains the doctrine of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى: قوله تعالى: {هَذَا رَبِّي} [الأنعام: ٧٦] والجواب: ذكر هذا على سبيل الفرض ليبطله بعد ذلك، كالواحد منا إذا أراد أن يبطل القول بقدم الأجسام، فيقول أولا: الجسم قديم أي هكذا يقول الخصم ثم يقول: لو كان قديما لم يكن متغيرا، فكذا ههنا: قال {هَذَا رَبِّي} أي كذا يقولون، ثم قال {لا أُحِبُّ الآفِلِينَ} [الأنعام: ٧٦] أي لو كان ربا لم يتغير.
The first objection: His saying, exalted: 'This is my Lord' (al-An'am 76). The answer: He mentioned this by way of hypothesis to refute it afterwards, like when one of us wants to refute the statement of the pre-eternity of bodies, he first says: 'The body is pre-eternal' – i.e., thus the opponent says – then he says: 'If it were pre-eternal, it would not be subject to change.' So similarly here: he said 'This is my Lord' meaning 'thus they say', then he said 'I do not like those that set' (al-An'am 76) meaning 'if He were a Lord, He would not change.'
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that Ibrahim (peace be upon him) attributed divinity to celestial bodies by saying 'This is my Lord.' The answer clarifies that this was not a declaration of belief but a rhetorical device – a hypothesis (farḍ) used to demonstrate the error of his people's beliefs. Razi draws an analogy: when a theologian refutes the pre-eternity of bodies, he first states the opponent's position ('the body is pre-eternal') and then refutes it. Similarly, Ibrahim first stated what the polytheists believed ('This is my Lord' – i.e., they say this) and then refuted it by saying 'I do not like those that set,' meaning that if the celestial body were truly a Lord, it would not be subject to change (setting). Thus, no actual attribution of divinity occurred.

Key Terms
فرض
استدلال
الآفلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibrahim actually believed a star was his Lord.
Correction: Ibrahim's statement was a hypothesis to refute his people's belief, not a personal declaration of faith.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' according to Razi?
A) It was a sincere belief
B) It was a hypothetical statement to refute later
C) It was a mistake
D) It was a test from Allah

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's statement was a rhetorical hypothesis meant to refute polytheism, not an endorsement of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share Islam with someone who holds a false belief, do you first thoroughly understand their position to state it accurately, or do you skip straight to your own argument? Will you commit this week to listening deeply before refuting?
?
Ibrahim's certainty allowed him to use a hypothesis without any fear of being misunderstood. How certain are you of your own faith? If your beliefs were tested by a skeptic who misrepresents them, would your response be calm like Ibrahim's or defensive?
?
If you were in Ibrahim's place, would you have the patience to use a teaching method for someone who worships the stars, or would you condemn immediately? What does your answer reveal about your own reliance on Allah's guidance?

The Objection of Ibrahim's Statement and Three Scholarly Answers
Razi provides three answers to the objection that Ibrahim falsely attributed idol-breaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational responses to the objection that Ibrahim (AS) lied when he said 'Rather, the largest of them did this'.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's statement was not a lie; it was rhetorical, causal, or interpretive.

In the previous lesson, we saw an objection about lying. Here, Imam Razi presents three scholarly answers to the objection that Ibrahim (AS) lied when he attributed the breaking of idols to the largest idol. Each answer provides a different plausible interpretation that avoids any accusation of falsehood, showing the depth of Islamic theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Focus on the first answer and its analogy about the calligrapher and the illiterate friend.
The first answer uses rhetorical style (تعريض) to explain Ibrahim's intent.
2
Understand the analogy of the illiterate writer
Reflect on how sarcasm works: attributing an action to someone who cannot do it is a form of mockery, not falsehood.
3
Examine the second answer about causal attribution
Notice that actions can be attributed to the motivating cause. Ibrahim's anger at the large idol led him to smash it, so he attributed the act to the idol.
This answer relies on the concept of إسناد (attribution).
4
Examine the third answer about grammatical pause
Consider the possibility of a pause (وقف) after 'بل فعله كبيرهم', making it a complete sentence referring to Ibrahim himself.
This interpretation relies on Arabic grammar.
5
Summarize the lesson's key takeaway
Review the three answers and state that each provides a valid way to understand Ibrahim's statement without falsehood.
Ibrahim's truthfulness is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: قال: {بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ} [الأنبياء: ٦٣] وهذا كذب. والجواب من وجوه: الأول: أن قصد إبراهيم ما كان أن ينسب ذلك الصادر عنه إلى الصنم، وإنما قصد تقريره لنفسه على أسلوب تعريضي به، وهذا كما لو قال صاحبك وقد كنت كتبت كتابا بخط حسن وأنت مشهور بحسن الخط: أنت كتبت هذا، وصاحبك أمي لا يحسن الخط ولا يقدر إلا على حروف خربشة فاسدة، فقلت له: بل كتبته أنت، وكان قصدك بهذا الجواب تقريره لك مع الاستهزاء به، لا نفيه عنك وإثباته للأمي؛ لأن إثباته والآمر دائر بينهما للعجز استهزاء به وإثبات للقادر. ثانيها: أن إبراهيم غاظته تلك الأصنام حين أبصرها مزينة، وكان غيظه من كبيرها أشد لما رأى من زيادة تعظيمهم له، فأسند الفعل إليه لأنه هو السبب في استهانته بها وحطمه لها، والفعل كما يسند إلى المباشر فقد يسند إلى السبب الحامل عليه. ثالثها: يجوز أن يكون فيه وقف عند قوله: {بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ} [الأنبياء: ٦٣] ثم يبتدئ فيقول: {فَاسْأَلُوهُمْ} [الأنبياء: ٦٣] والمعنى: بل فعله كبيرهم، وعنى به نفسه؛ لأن الإنسان أكبر من كل صنم.
The second objection: He said: 'Rather, their largest did this' (al-Anbiya 63). And this is a lie. The answer is from aspects: The first: That the intention of Ibrahim was not to attribute that which issued from him to the idol, but rather he intended its ascription to himself in a rhetorical style. This is like when your companion – while you had written a book in good handwriting and you are famous for good handwriting – says: 'You wrote this,' and your companion is illiterate, cannot write well, and is only capable of scribbled rotten letters, so you say to him: 'Rather, you wrote it.' Your intention with this answer is to affirm it for yourself while mocking him, not to deny it from yourself and affirm it for the illiterate; because affirming it while the matter is confined between the two due to inability is mockery of him and affirmation of the capable. The second: That Ibrahim was angered by those idols when he saw them adorned, and his anger at their largest was more intense due to what he saw of their increased veneration of it, so he attributed the action to it because it was the cause of his contempt for them and his smashing them. Actions, just as they are attributed to the direct agent, may also be attributed to the motivating cause. The third: It is possible that there is a pause at His saying: 'Rather, their largest did this' (al-Anbiya 63), then he begins saying: 'So ask them' (al-Anbiya 63), and the meaning: 'Rather, their largest did this,' and he meant himself; because man is greater than any idol.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection is that Ibrahim's statement 'Rather, the largest of them did this' is a lie because he himself broke the idols. Razi gives three answers. (1) Ibrahim intended to mock his people by rhetorically attributing the act to the large idol, similar to sarcastically praising an illiterate person for beautiful writing. (2) Ibrahim was angered by the idols and attributed the action to the large idol because it was the cause of his anger, as actions can be attributed to the motivating cause. (3) There may be a grammatical pause after 'بل فعله كبيرهم' and then a new sentence 'فاسألوهم' meaning 'So ask them', and 'their large one' refers to Ibrahim himself because he is greater than any idol.

Key Terms
تعريض
إسناد
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ibrahim blatantly lied when he said 'بل فعله كبيرهم'.
Correction: The passage shows multiple plausible interpretations that avoid falsehood: rhetorical sarcasm, causal attribution, or a grammatical pause referring to himself.

Checkpoint
How many answers does Razi give to the objection of Ibrahim's statement 'بل فعله كبيرهم'?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's statement 'بل فعله كبيرهم' is not a lie; it has valid interpretations as rhetorical sarcasm, causal attribution, or a grammatical pause referring to himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where the truth is on your side but you need to speak wisely, do you have the self-control to use rhetorical skill like Ibrahim (AS) did, or does your anger or ego take over? Reflect on a recent conversation where you could have been more tactful. What will you do differently next time?
?
Ibrahim (AS) was willing to be seen as saying something that could be misinterpreted rather than betray tawheed. Are there areas in your life where you stay silent about Allah's oneness out of fear of being called extreme or dishonest? Write down one situation before next week where you will speak up for tawheed even if it means using clever wording.
?
The third answer says Ibrahim (AS) meant himself when he said 'their large one' because a human is greater than any idol. Do you truly believe in your heart that you are greater than any created thing that people worship today—like fame, money, or status? What steps will you take this week to ensure your actions reflect that belief?
